Ruling on Praying Towards a Candle or Lamp
Commentary explaining that praying towards a candle or lamp is not disliked

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying towards a candle or lamp and explain the reasoning that the act is not disliked because the object is not worshipped.

Core Idea
A candle or lamp is not an object of worship, so facing it in prayer is not disliked.

This lesson examines a specific ruling about the direction of prayer. The Hanafi school clarifies that the mere presence of a candle or lamp in the direction of prayer does not render the prayer disliked (makruh). The key distinction is whether the object itself is venerated. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the reasoning and resolves a difference of opinion among scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its initial ruling
The matn states 'or a candle or lamp' in the context of what is disliked to face in prayer. The commentator clarifies that facing them is not disliked.
The passage begins by explaining the reason.
2
Analyze the reasoning (talil)
The dislike is based on the object being worshipped. Since candles and lamps are not objects of worship, there is no dislike.
The Magians worship fire only in a specific context (hearth with embers).
3
Note the ikhtilaf among Hanafi scholars
Some earlier scholars considered it makruh, but the preferred view (mukhtar) is that it is not disliked.
The author cites Ghayat al-Bayan and chooses the view of no karaha.
4
Understand the practical application
The absence of dislike is agreed upon when candles are placed on the sides, as is customary in Egypt during Ramadan for tarawih prayers.
This shows how custom ('urf) can affect the application of rulings.
5
Linguistic note
The word 'sham' (candle) has two pronunciations; the more correct is with a fatha on the mim.
Though not central, it reflects the meticulousness of Hanafi scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (أو) شمع أو سراج) لأنهما لا يعبدان والكراهة باعتبارها وإنما يعبدها المجوس إذا كانت في الكانون وفيها الجمر أو في التنور فلا يكره التوجه إليها على غير هذا الوجه، وذكر في غاية البيان اختلاف المشايخ في التوجه إلى الشمع أو السراج والمختار أنه لا يكره ا هـ. وينبغي أن يكون عدم الكراهة متفقاً عليه فيما إذا كان الشمع على جانبيه هو المعتاد في مصر المحروسة في ليالي رمضان للتراويح. قال ابن قتيبة في أدب الكاتب في باب ما جاء فيه لغتان: استعمل الناس أضعفهما الشمع بالسكون والأوجه فتح الميم.
His statement: (Or) a candle or lamp) because they are not worshipped, and dislike is based on that. The Magians only worship them when they are in a lampstand with embers or in an oven, so it is not disliked to face them in other forms. It is mentioned in Ghayat al-Bayan that there is disagreement among the mashayikh regarding facing a candle or lamp, and the chosen view is that it is not disliked. And it should be that the absence of dislike is agreed upon when the candle is on the sides, as is customary in Egypt during Ramadan nights for tarawih. Ibn Qutayba said in Adab al-Katib in the chapter on words with two pronunciations: People use the weaker one, 'sham' with sukūn, and the more correct is with fatḥa on the mīm.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on praying towards a candle or lamp. The matn states 'or a candle or lamp', and the commentator explains that it is not disliked because these objects are not worshipped; dislike would only apply if they were objects of worship. The Magians worship fire, but only when it is in a hearth with burning coals; otherwise, the object itself is not venerated. The commentator notes that there is a difference of opinion among scholars, but the preferred (mukhtar) view is that it is not disliked. He adds that in the custom of Egypt during Ramadan, placing candles on the sides is not disliked at all. A linguistic note clarifies the correct pronunciation of 'sham' (candle).

Key Terms
شمع (sham'): A candle, but the text discusses the pronunciation; the correct spelling and pronunciation are noted.
سراج (siraj): A lamp or light source
كانون (kanun): A brazier or hearth where fire is lit

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi ruling on praying towards a candle or lamp?
A) It is makruh tanzihi
B) It is makruh tahrimi
C) It is not disliked
D) It is haram

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, praying towards a candle or lamp is not disliked because the object is not worshipped; the general principle is that only objects of veneration render the prayer makruh.

Ruling on praying on a mat with images
Commentary on praying on a mat with images: not disliked if not prostrating on them

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying on a mat with images and identify the condition that makes it permissible.

Core Idea
Prayer on a mat with images is fine as long as you don't prostrate on them.

This short passage from Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a common concern: can you pray on a prayer mat that has pictures? The Hanafi school permits it, but with a precise condition. Let's examine the exact wording and its implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is being ruled upon? It is the act of praying on a mat that contains images. The ruling is that it is 'not disliked' (i.e., permissible or mubah).
The Arabic says 'لا يكره' (it is not disliked).
2
Note the condition
The permissibility is conditional: 'if he does not prostrate on them' (إن لم يسجد عليها). This means the images must not be under the prostration spots (forehead, nose, hands, knees, feet).
Prostration (sujud) is the key element.
3
Trace the source of the condition
The commentator says this restriction is based on Imam Muhammad's al-Jami' al-Saghir, a foundational Hanafi text. This shows the ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah.
References to earlier discussions are not repeated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى بساط فيه تصاوير إن لم يسجد عليها أي لا يكره. والتقييد المذكور بناء على ما في الجامع الصغير وقد قدمنا مفهومه وما في الأصل فلا حاجة إلى إعادته.
And on a mat with images, if he does not prostrate on them, meaning it is not disliked. And the aforementioned restriction is based on what is in al-Jami' al-Saghir, and we have already presented its import and what is in the original text, so there is no need to repeat it.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a brief ruling. The commentator explains the matn's phrase: praying on a mat that has images (pictures of animate beings) is not disliked provided the worshipper does not prostrate directly onto the images. This condition is taken from Imam Muhammad's al-Jami' al-Saghir. The commentator notes that the concept and the original ruling have already been discussed earlier, so he does not elaborate further.

Key Terms
بساط (bisat): A mat or carpet used for prayer
تصاوير (tasawir): Images, specifically of animate beings that cast shadows? The context implies general images.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The images must be of inanimate objects
B) The worshipper must not prostrate directly on the images
C) The images must be covered during prayer
D) The mat must be plain with no images

Key Takeaway
Prayer on a mat with images is permissible in Hanafi fiqh as long as the worshipper does not prostrate directly on the images.

Additional Disliked Acts in Prayer
Enumeration and discussion of various disliked acts in prayer not covered by the author

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the additional disliked acts in prayer mentioned in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, distinguishing between makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzih based on the strength of the sunna.

Core Idea
Omitting an emphasized sunna is makruh tahrimi; omitting a regular sunna is makruh tanzih; mustahabb omission is not disliked.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the makruhat (disliked acts) in prayer that the author of Kanz al-Daqaiq did not list. The commentator first establishes a general principle regarding the severity of leaving a sunna, then enumerates specific examples from various Hanafi sources. Understanding these nuances helps refine one's prayer practice and avoid unnecessary sin.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general principle
The commentator explains that omitting an emphasized sunna (sunna mu'akkada) is makruh tahrimi, akin to leaving a wajib. Omitting a non-emphasized sunna is makruh tanzih. Omitting a mustahabb is not disliked at all.
This principle underlies all subsequent examples.
2
Identify posture and adhkar related disliked acts
The first set includes placing hands on the ground before knees in sujud, raising head in ruku', saying basmalah and amin aloud, not placing hands properly, omitting tasbihat, reducing tasbihat, and performing transition adhkar after the movement.
These are all makruh tanzih unless the associated sunna is mu'akkada.
3
Examine recitation-specific disliked acts and the intra-Hanafi dispute
Reciting the end of a surah in each rak'ah separately is makruh according to most, but Qadikhan corrected that it is not makruh. Jumping between verses/surahs in one rak'ah is makruh; in two rak'ahs, the ruling depends on the gap.
This shows the nuance within the school.
4
Note the distinction between fard and nafl prayers
All these rulings apply only to obligatory (fard) prayers. In nafl prayers, these acts are not disliked.
This is an important exception.
5
Review remaining acts
Switching surahs mid-recitation after one or two verses is makruh, even if less than a verse is recited. Praying in work clothes is also disliked, based on a narration from 'Umar.
The last act is supported by a specific hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن المصنف لم يستوف ذكر المكروهات في الصلاة فمنها أن كل سنة تركها فهو مكروه تنزيهاً صرح به في منية المصلي من قوله ويكره وضع اليدين على الأرض قبل الركبتين إذا سجد ورفعهما قبلهما إذا قام إلا من عذر، وأن يرفع رأسه أو ينكسه في الركوع، وأن يجهر بالتسمية والتأمين، وأن لا يضع يديه في موضعهما إلا من عذر، وأن يترك التسبيحات في الركوع والسجود، وأن ينقص من ثلاث تسبيحات في الركوع والسجود، وأن يأتي بالأذكار المشروعة في الانتقالات بعد تمام الانتقال وفيه خللان تركها في موضعها وتحصيلها في غير موضعها. ذكره في مواضع متفرقة من مكروهات الصلاة وحاصله أن السنة إذا كانت مؤكدة قوية لا يبعد أن يكون تركها مكروهاً كراهة تحريم كترك الواجب فإنه كذلك، كانت غير مؤكدة فتركها مكروه تنزيهاً كما في هذه الأمثلة وإن كان ذلك الشيء مستحباً أو مندوباً وليس بسنة كما هو على اصطلاحنا فينبغي أن لا يكون تركه مكروهاً أصلاً كما صرحوا به من يستحب يوم الأضحى أن لا يأكل أوّلاً إلا من أضحيته. قالوا: ولو أكل من غيرها فليس بمكروه فلم يلزم من ترك المستحب ثبوت كراهته إلا أنه يشكل عليه ما قالوه أن المكروه تنزيهاً مرجعه إلى خلاف الأولى، ولا شك أن ترك المستحب خلاف الأولى . من ومنها ما في الخلاصة والولوالجية ولا ينبغي أن يقرأ في كل ركعة آخر سورة على حدة فإن مكروه عند الأكثر وينبغي أن يقرأ في الركعتين آخر سورة واحدة وهو أفضل من السورة إن كان الآخر أكثر آية ا هـ . وصحح قاضيخان في شرح الجامع الصغير عدم الكراهة وإن كان الأفضل خلافه. ومنها الانتقال من آية من سورة إلى آية أخرى من سورة أخرى أو آية من هذه السورة بينهما آيات وكذا الجمع بين السورتين بينهما سور أو سورة واحدة في ركعة واحدة مكروه وفي الركعتين إن كان بينهما سور لا يكره وإن كان بينهما سورة واحدة قال بعضهم يكره، وقال بعضهم إن كانت السورة طويلة لا يكره كما إذا كانت بينهما سورتان قصيرتان. ومنها أن يقرأ في ركعة أخرى سورة وفي ركعة أخرى سورة فوق تلك السورة أو فعل ذلك في ركعة فهو مكروه، وإن وقع هذا من غير قصد بأن قرأ في الركعة الأولى «قل أعود برب الناس» يقرأ في الركعة الثانية هذه السورة أيضاً. وهذا كله في الفرائض، أما في النوافل لا يكره كذا في الخلاصة. ومنها ما إذا افتتح سورة وقصده سورة أخرى فلما قرأ آية أو آيتين أراد أن يترك تلك السورة ويفتتح التي أرادها يكره، وكذا لو قرأ أقل من آية وإن كان حرفاً. ومنها أن يصلي في ثياب البذلة والمهنة واحتج له في الذخيرة بأنه روي عن عمر رضي الله عنه
Then know that the author did not exhaust the mention of disliked acts in prayer. Among them: that every sunna whose omission is makruh tanzih, as stated in Munyat al-Musalli: it is disliked to place the hands on the ground before the knees when prostrating and to raise them before the knees when standing up except due to an excuse; to raise one's head or lower it in ruku'; to pronounce the basmalah and amin aloud; not to place his hands in their proper place except due to an excuse; to leave the tasbihat in ruku' and sujud; to reduce from three tasbihat in ruku' and sujud; to perform the prescribed adhkar for transitions after completing the transition—this contains two faults: leaving them in their proper place and performing them out of place. This is mentioned in various places regarding disliked acts in prayer. The summary: if the sunna is firmly established (mu'akkada), it is not unlikely that leaving it is makruh tahrimi, like leaving a wajib; if it is not firmly established, leaving it is makruh tanzih, as in these examples. If the thing is mustahabb or mandub and not sunna according to our terminology, its omission should not be disliked at all, as they explicitly state: it is mustahabb on the day of Adha to not eat first except from one's sacrifice. They said: if one eats from something else, it is not makruh, so leaving mustahabb does not entail dislike. However, there is a problem: they say that makruh tanzih returns to khilaf al-awla (contrary to what is better), and there is no doubt that leaving mustahabb is khilaf al-awla. Among them: what is in al-Khulasa and al-Walwaljiyya: one should not recite the end of a surah separately in each rak'ah, for it is makruh according to most. One should recite the end of one surah in two rak'ahs, and that is better than [reading] the surah if the latter has more verses. Qadikhan corrected in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir that there is no makruh, though the opposite is better. Among them: moving from a verse of one surah to another verse of another surah or to a verse of this surah with verses in between, likewise combining two surahs with surahs in between or one surah in a single rak'ah is makruh; in two rak'ahs, if there are surahs in between, it is not makruh; if there is one surah between, some say it is makruh, others say the long surah is not makruh as if there are two short surahs in between. Among them: reciting in one rak'ah a surah and in another rak'ah a surah above that surah, or doing that in one rak'ah, is makruh. If this occurs unintentionally, such that he recited 'Qul A'udhu bi Rabbi an-Nas' in the first rak'ah, he recites this same surah in the second rak'ah. All this is in obligatory prayers; in nafl prayers it is not makruh, as in al-Khulasa. Among them: if one begins a surah intending another surah, then after reciting one or two verses wants to leave that surah and begin the intended one, it is makruh; likewise if he recited less than a verse, even a letter. Among them: praying in clothes of work and occupation (bahlah and mihnah), as argued in al-Dhakhirah on the authority of 'Umar radiya Allahu 'anhu.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive listing of various disliked acts (makruhat) in prayer that the author of Kanz al-Daqaiq did not include. The commentator first establishes a general principle: omitting an emphasized sunna (sunna mu'akkada) may be makruh tahrimi, while omitting a non-emphasized sunna is makruh tanzih. Acts that are merely mustahabb or mandub are not disliked if omitted. Then he enumerates specific examples: placing hands on ground before knees in sujud; raising hands before knees when standing; lifting or lowering the head in ruku'; saying basmalah and amin aloud; not placing hands correctly; missing tasbihat in ruku' and sujud; reducing the number of tasbihat; performing transition adhkar after the movement. Then he discusses recitation issues: reading the end of a surah separately in each rak'ah (disliked by most, but Qadikhan corrected that it is not makruh), jumping between verses and surahs in one rak'ah (disliked), and combining surahs in one rak'ah with large gaps (disliked). In two rak'ahs, the ruling varies: if multiple surahs between, not makruh; if one surah between, disagreement. Reciting a surah in one rak'ah and then a higher-numbered surah in the next is makruh unless unintentional. These rulings apply to fard prayers; in nafl, no karaha. Also, starting a surah with a different intention and switching after one or two verses is makruh. Finally, praying in work clothes is disliked, citing a narration from 'Umar. The passage combines rulings from multiple sources like Munyat al-Musalli, al-Khulasa, al-Walwaljiyya, Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir, and al-Dhakhirah.

Key Terms
سنة مؤكدة (sunna mu'akkada): An emphasized sunnah; its omission is makruh tahrimi according to some, but here the commentator says 'لا يبعد أن يكون مكروهاً كراهة تحريم'.
مكروه تنزيهاً (makruh tanzihi): A disliked act that is not prohibited; its commission is not sinful but contrary to better practice.
مكروه تحريماً (makruh tahrimi): A disliked act close to haram; its commission is sinful, though not as severe as haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered makruh tahrimi?
A) Leaving a sunna mu'akkada
B) Leaving a mustahabb
C) Reciting aloud in nafl
D) Praying in work clothes

Key Takeaway
Disliked acts in prayer vary in severity; omitting an emphasized sunna is closer to prohibition, while omitting a regular sunna is only tanzihi, and mustahabb omissions are not disliked.

Dislike of Leaving Sunnah and Mustahabb Acts
Classification of makruh levels for omitted sunnah and mustahabb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three levels of disliked acts (makruh tahrimi, makruh tanzihi, khilaf al-awla) and apply them to sunnah and mustahabb acts.

Core Idea
Leaving sunnah mu'akkadah is severely disliked; leaving mustahabb is only contrary to preference, not disliked.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, the author clarifies subtle distinctions within the category of makruh (disliked) in the Hanafi school. Students often assume any omission of a recommended act is disliked, but the Hanafi classification is more nuanced, separating sunnah mu'akkadah, ghayr mu'akkadah, and mustahabb. The passage also raises a potential contradiction regarding the definition of makruh tanzihi as khilaf al-awla.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of recommended acts
Recognize that the text mentions sunnah mu'akkadah (strongly emphasized), sunnah ghayr mu'akkadah (non-emphasized), and mustahabb/mandub (recommended but not sunnah).
2
Determine the ruling for omitting each category
From the text: leaving sunnah mu'akkadah is makruh tahrimi; leaving sunnah ghayr mu'akkadah is makruh tanzihi; leaving mustahabb is not makruh but is khilaf al-awla.
Note the contrast: only sunnah omissions are disliked; mustahabb omission is not inherently disliked.
3
Recognize the author's objection
The author points out that if makruh tanzihi is defined as khilaf al-awla, then leaving mustahabb (which is clearly khilaf al-awla) should also be makruh tanzihi. This is unresolved in this passage.
This objection shows careful reasoning within the Hanafi tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة باب ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها أنه وإن المشروعة في الانتقالات بعد تمام الانتقال وفيه خللان تركها في موضعها وتحصيلها في غير موضعها. ذكره في مواضع متفرقة من مكروهات الصلاة وحاصله أن السنة إذا كانت مؤكدة قوية لا يبعد أن يكون تركها مكروهاً كراهة تحريم كترك الواجب فإنه كذلك، كانت غير مؤكدة فتركها مكروه تنزيهاً كما في هذه الأمثلة وإن كان ذلك الشيء مستحباً أو مندوباً وليس بسنة كما هو على اصطلاحنا فينبغي أن لا يكون تركه مكروهاً أصلاً كما صرحوا به من يستحب يوم الأضحى أن لا يأكل أوّلاً إلا من أضحيته. قالوا: ولو أكل من غيرها فليس بمكروه فلم يلزم من ترك المستحب ثبوت كراهته إلا أنه يشكل عليه ما قالوه أن المكروه تنزيهاً مرجعه إلى خلاف الأولى، ولا شك أن ترك المستحب خلاف الأولى .
Book of Prayer, Chapter of What Invalidates Prayer and What Is Disliked in It. He said: And although the prescribed acts in transitions after completing the transition, there are two faults: leaving it in its place and achieving it in other than its place. It is mentioned in scattered places of the disliked acts of prayer. The summary is that if the sunnah is strongly emphasized, it is not unlikely that leaving it is disliked with the dislike of prohibition (makruh tahrimi) like leaving an obligatory act, for it is such. If it is not emphasized, then leaving it is disliked with tanzihi dislike, as in these examples. And if that thing is mustahabb or mandub and not sunnah, as per our terminology, then leaving it should not be disliked at all, as they explicitly stated regarding the one for whom it is mustahabb on the day of Adha not to eat first except from his sacrifice. They said: If he eats from other than it, it is not disliked. So it does not follow that leaving the mustahabb establishes its dislike. However, it is problematic what they said: that the tanzihi dislike returns to khilaf al-awla, and there is no doubt that leaving the mustahabb is khilaf al-awla.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and definition of the legal classification of disliked acts (makruh) in relation to sunnah, mustahabb, and mandub. The author explains that the degree of dislike depends on the strength of the sunnah: leaving a mu'akkadah sunnah can be makruh tahrimi (like leaving a wajib), while leaving a non-mu'akkadah sunnah is makruh tanzihi. As for mustahabb or mandub acts, their omission is generally not disliked at all, though it is 'khilaf al-awla' (contrary to what is better). The author raises an objection: if tanzihi dislike is defined as khilaf al-awla, then leaving mustahabb should also be makruh tanzihi. This objection is not resolved here; it is addressed later in the text.

Key Terms
سنة مؤكدة (sunnah mu'akkadah): A confirmed sunnah, leaving which is severely disliked
مكروه تحريماً (makruh tahriman): Dislike approaching prohibition, almost as sinful as leaving a wajib
خلاف الأولى (khilaf al-awla): Contrary to what is preferable, but not necessarily disliked

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on leaving a sunnah mu'akkadah?
A) It is permissible without dislike.
B) It is makruh tahrimi.
C) It is haram.
D) It is wajib.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the ruling on omitting a recommended act depends on its strength: sunnah mu'akkadah omission is makruh tahrimi, ghayr mu'akkadah is makruh tanzihi, and mustahabb omission is only khilaf al-awla, not disliked per se.

Makruh Recitations in Prayer
Lists disliked recitation practices

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify four categories of makruh recitations in Hanafi fiqh and explain the ruling for each.

Core Idea
Random surah transitions and changing intention mid-recitation are makruh tanzihi in fard prayers, but not in nafl.

The Hanafi school places great emphasis on order and intentionality in Qur’anic recitation during prayer. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq details several actions that, while not invalidating the prayer, are considered disliked (makruh) because they compromise the reverence and sequence established by the Sunnah. Understanding these distinctions helps the worshipper avoid what is blameworthy and perfect their prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
For each point in the passage, name the specific recitation practice and determine whether it is makruh or not disliked according to the majority.
Pay attention to exceptions like nafl prayers and unintentional occurrences.
2
Recognize intra-madhhab differences
Note that Qadikhan disagrees with the majority on one point—reciting separate surah endings—and consider why his view is not the relied-upon one.
The majority view is mufta bihi.
3
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a novel case (e.g., switching between verses of the same surah), deduce whether it would be makruh based on the underlying principle (disruption of order).
The principle is that any recitation that breaks the natural progression of the Qur’an is disliked in fard prayers.
4
Distinguish between fard and nafl
Note that all these rulings apply only to obligatory prayers; in supererogatory prayers they are not disliked.
This is explicitly stated in the text from al-Khulasa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ما في الخلاصة والولوالجية ولا ينبغي أن يقرأ في كل ركعة آخر سورة على حدة فإن مكروه عند الأكثر وينبغي أن يقرأ في الركعتين آخر سورة واحدة وهو أفضل من السورة إن كان الآخر أكثر آية ا هـ . وصحح قاضيخان في شرح الجامع الصغير عدم الكراهة وإن كان الأفضل خلافه. ومنها الانتقال من آية من سورة إلى آية أخرى من سورة أخرى أو آية من هذه السورة بينهما آيات وكذا الجمع بين السورتين بينهما سور أو سورة واحدة في ركعة واحدة مكروه وفي الركعتين إن كان بينهما سور لا يكره وإن كان بينهما سورة واحدة قال بعضهم يكره، وقال بعضهم إن كانت السورة طويلة لا يكره كما إذا كانت بينهما سورتان قصيرتان. ومنها أن يقرأ في ركعة أخرى سورة وفي ركعة أخرى سورة فوق تلك السورة أو فعل ذلك في ركعة فهو مكروه، وإن وقع هذا من غير قصد بأن قرأ في الركعة الأولى «قل أعود برب الناس» يقرأ في الركعة الثانية هذه السورة أيضاً. وهذا كله في الفرائض، أما في النوافل لا يكره كذا في الخلاصة. ومنها ما إذا افتتح سورة وقصده سورة أخرى فلما قرأ آية أو آيتين أراد أن يترك تلك السورة ويفتتح التي أرادها يكره، وكذا لو قرأ أقل من آية وإن كان حرفاً.
Among them is what is in al-Khulasa and al-Walwaljiyya: It is not proper to recite in every rakah the last part of a separate surah, for it is disliked according to the majority; rather one should recite in the two rakahs the last part of one surah, and that is better than reciting a surah if that last part has more verses. Qadikhan corrected in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir that there is no dislike, though the better is otherwise. Among them: Transitioning from a verse of one surah to another verse of another surah, or a verse from this surah between which there are verses; likewise, joining two surahs between which there are surahs or one surah in one rakah is disliked. In two rakahs, if between them there are surahs, it is not disliked; if between them there is one surah, some said it is disliked, some said if the surah is long it is not disliked, like if between them there are two short surahs. Among them: Reciting in another rakah a surah, and in another rakah a surah above that surah, or doing that in one rakah is disliked. If this happens unintentionally, like reciting 'Qul A'udhu bi Rabbi al-Nas' in the first rakah, then reciting that same surah in the second rakah. All this applies to obligatory prayers; as for supererogatory prayers, it is not disliked, as in al-Khulasa. Among them: If one starts a surah intending another surah, then after reciting one or two verses wants to leave that surah and start the intended one, it is disliked. Likewise if he recited less than a verse, even a letter.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents multiple disliked (makruh) acts related to recitation during obligatory prayers. The first example is reading the final portion of a surah separately in each rakah; the majority consider this makruh, though Qadikhan corrects that there is no dislike, but the better is not to do it. The second example is arbitrary transition between verses of different surahs, or combining two surahs with gaps, which is makruh in one rakah but may be permissible in two rakahs depending on the number of intervening surahs. The third example is reciting a surah in a later rakah that is higher in order than the one in the first rakah, which is makruh unless unintentional. The fourth example is changing one's intention to a different surah after starting, which is makruh even if only a few letters have been recited. The author notes that these rulings apply only to fard prayers; in nafl prayers, they are not disliked.

Key Terms
الانتقال من آية إلى أخرى (al-intiqal min ayah ila ukhra): Switching between verses of different surahs during recitation
الجمع بين السورتين (al-jam' bayn al-surahtayn): Reciting two surahs together in one rakah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT considered makruh during fard prayer?
A) Reciting a surah in the second rakah that is higher in order than the one in the first rakah, done intentionally.
B) Reciting the last part of a separate surah in each rakah.
C) Reciting a verse from Surah al-Fatiha then a verse from Surah al-Baqarah in the same rakah (after Fatiha).
D) Starting to recite one surah, then after one verse deciding to recite a different surah.

Key Takeaway
To preserve the reverence and order of prayer, avoid arbitrary verse transitions, combining surahs with gaps, and changing surah intention mid-recitation in fard prayers.

The Ruling on Praying in Work Clothes
Ruling on praying in work clothes with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying in work clothes and explain the evidence from the practice of Umar and the hadith.

Core Idea
Praying in shabby work clothes is makruh tanzihi — dignity in prayer reflects dignity before Allah.

In this lesson, we examine a specific aspect of prayer etiquette: the clothing one wears. The Hanafi school, drawing on the practice of Umar and a prophetic hadith, considers it disliked to pray in everyday work clothes. But what kind of dislike? The passage clarifies the degree and provides the textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? (praying in work clothes) and what is the hukm? (makruh tanzihi). Note that the ruling applies to clothing considered 'shabby' or 'work clothes', explained as garments worn at home, not suitable for visiting dignitaries.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites two proofs: an incident from Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) and a hadith narrated by al-Bayhaqi. Identify each as a Companions opinion (qawl sahabi) and a hadith, respectively.
In Hanafi usul, qawl sahabi is considered a proof when it does not contradict qiyas or other stronger evidence.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
How does each proof lead to the ruling? Umars analogy: if you wouldn't wear these clothes to a human dignitary, then Allah is more deserving of adornment. The hadith commands: 'When one of you prays, let him wear his two garments.' The command 'falyalbas' is understood as recommendation (nadb) due to the context of adorning for Allah, not obligation.
The imperative form ('liyalbas') generally indicates obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul, but is overridden here by contextual indicators (qara'in) showing it is a matter of etiquette.
4
Determine the degree of dislike
The author explicitly states 'al-Zahir annaha tanzihiyyah' — it appears to be tanzihi dislike (not sinful but discouraged). This is the mufta bihi position. Some might consider it tahrimi, but the evidence supports the milder view.
Hanafi scholars differentiate between makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked, akin to haram) and makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked, but still valid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن يصلي في ثياب البذلة والمهنة واحتج له في الذخيرة بأنه روي عن عمر رضي الله عنه أنه رأى رجلاً فعل ذلك فقال : أرأيتك لو كنت أرسلتك إلى بعض الناس أكنت تمر في ثيابك هذه؟ فقال لا . فقال عمر: الله أحق أن يتزين له. وروى البيهقي عنه صلى الله عليه وسلم «إذا صلى أحدكم فليلبس ثوبيه فإن الله أحق من أن يتزين له والظاهر أنها تنزيهية. وفسر ثياب البذلة في شرح الوقاية بما يلبسه في بيته ولا يذهب به إلى الأكابر
Among them: That one prays in shabby and work clothes. He argued for it in al-Dhakhira based on the narration from Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that he saw a man doing that and said: 'Do you think, if I had sent you to some people, would you pass in these clothes?' He said: 'No.' Then Umar said: 'Allah is more deserving that you adorn yourself for Him.' And al-Bayhaqi narrated from him (peace be upon him): 'When one of you prays, let him wear his two garments, for Allah is more deserving that you adorn yourself for Him.' It appears that it is tanzihi dislike. And he interpreted 'thiyab al-badhlah' in Sharh al-Wiqayah as what one wears at home and does not wear when going to dignitaries.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that praying in everyday work clothes (thiyab al-badhlah) is disliked (makruh), and it provides proof from the practice of Umar (a Companion's opinion, considered a proof in Hanafi usul) and a hadith from al-Bayhaqi. The author notes that this dislike is tanzihi (not prohibition-level). The definition of work clothes is clarified as garments worn at home, not suitable for visiting important people. The underlying principle is that one should dress properly for prayer as an act of respect for Allah.

Key Terms
ثياب البذلة (thiyab al-badhlah): Work clothes or shabby garments worn at home, not fit for formal occasions

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A verse from the Quran
B) An incident from Umar's life and a hadith
C) Consensus of scholars
D) Logical reasoning

Key Takeaway
Praying in shabby work clothes is valid but disliked (makruh tanzih) based on the practice of Umar and a hadith.

Carrying a Child in Prayer
Ruling on carrying a child in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on carrying a child during prayer and explain why the Prophet's action is not a valid proof of permissibility.

Core Idea
Carrying a child in prayer is makruh tanzih; the Prophet's carrying of Umamah is abrogated.

When the Prophet (peace be upon him) carried his granddaughter Umamah during prayer, some might see this as permission. However, Hanafi scholars explain that this action was abrogated by the command that prayer demands full attention. This lesson examines the ruling and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is carrying a child during the performance of salah. The Hanafi ruling is that it is makruh tanzih (disliked, not sinful).
2
Examine the evidence
The Prophet once carried Umamah during prayer. This action is cited as potential evidence for permissibility. However, Hanafis argue that this specific action is abrogated (mansukh) by the general command that prayer is a serious occupation.
The hadith 'Indeed in prayer there is occupation' is used to support abrogation.
3
Understand the reasoning
The abrogation argument prioritizes the general principle of concentration in prayer over the specific prophetic action. Carrying a child is a form of distraction, thus disliked.
Al-Halabi discussed this at length, as noted in the text.
4
Distinguish the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that the ruling of makruh is based on the overriding principle of avoiding distractions, and the prophetic example does not override that due to abrogation.
Some other schools may consider it permissible based on the prophetic action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن يحمل صبياً في صلاته، وأما حمله صلى الله عليه وسلم أمامة بنت زينب في الصلاة فأجيب عنه بوجوه. منها: أنه منسوخ بقوله أن في الصلاة لشغلاً. وقد أطال الكلام فيه العلامة الحلبي. ومنها أن يضع في فيه دراهم أو دنانير بحيث لا تمنعه عن القراءة وإن منعه عن أداء الحروف لا يجوز كما الخلاصة وغيرها.
Among them: Carrying a child in his prayer. As for the Prophet (peace be upon him) carrying Umamah bint Zainab in prayer, it is answered in several ways: among them, it is abrogated by his statement 'Indeed in prayer there is occupation.' Al-Halabi has discussed this at length. Among them: Placing dirhams or dinars in his mouth such that they do not prevent him from recitation; if they prevent him from articulating letters, it is not permissible, as in al-Khulasa and others.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct disliked acts. First, carrying a child during prayer is generally considered makruh. The Prophet's action of carrying Umamah is explained as either abrogated or specific to him. Second, placing coins in the mouth is permissible only if it does not hinder the proper recitation of letters; if it does, it is not allowed. The source for the latter is al-Khulasa.

Key Terms
منسوخ (mansukh): Abrogated; the ruling of permissibility in the Prophet's action is superseded
مكروه تنزيهي (makruh tanzih): Disliked but not sinful; omission is rewarded but commission is not punished

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school considers carrying a child in prayer as makruh tanzih, and the Prophet's carrying of Umamah is abrogated by the command of concentration in prayer.

Placing Coins in the Mouth During Prayer
Ruling on placing coins in the mouth during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which placing coins in the mouth during prayer becomes prohibited.

Core Idea
Placing coins in the mouth is permissible as long as it does not hinder proper recitation; if it does, it is not allowed.

Prayer requires correct articulation of Quranic letters. Placing objects like coins in the mouth can interfere with this. This lesson clarifies the boundary between permissible and prohibited, based on the source al-Khulasa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The act is placing dirhams or dinars in the mouth during prayer. The default ruling is that it is permissible (ja'iz) provided a condition is met.
2
Determine the condition
The coins must not prevent the proper articulation of letters (huruf). As long as they do not obstruct recitation, placing them is allowed.
The text says 'بحيث لا تمنعه عن القراءة' (such that they do not prevent him from recitation).
3
Identify the prohibited case
If the coins prevent the articulation of letters, the act becomes prohibited (la yajuz). The text explicitly states that this is not permissible.
The source for this ruling is al-Khulasa and others.
4
Understand the legal consequence
Since recitation is wajib in prayer, preventing it may invalidate the prayer or require compensation (sajdah sahw) if unintentional.

Source Text

Rulings on Reciting in Ruku and Standing Alone Behind the Row
Two rulings on disliked acts in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for reciting Quran in ruku and for praying alone behind the row, and identify the condition that makes the latter permissible.

Core Idea
Both acts are makruh tanzih; standing alone is allowed only if no gap exists.

This lesson examines two specific disliked actions in prayer: reciting Quran during ruku and standing alone behind the congregation as a follower. While both are considered makruh tanzih (slightly disliked) in the Hanafi school, the second has a clear exception when there is no gap in the row. Understanding these rulings helps preserve the proper adab of salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disliked acts
The passage explicitly lists two actions: completing recitation in ruku and standing alone behind the row as a follower. Note that the first act includes reciting in any non-standing posture.
The phrase 'from them' indicates these are among the disliked acts mentioned earlier.
2
State the ruling for each act
Both are ruled as makruh tanzih (slightly disliked). However, the second act has an exception: it becomes permissible if there is no gap in the row.
Makruh tanzih means the act is not sinful but should be avoided for optimal worship.
3
Identify the condition for the second act
The text says 'unless he does not find a gap'. This condition removes the dislike; if a gap exists, standing alone remains makruh.
The first act has no such condition; it is always disliked.
4
Apply the rulings to practice
When following an imam, one should join a row if possible. If no gap is found, standing alone is allowed but still makruh. Recitation should be confined to qiyam, so reciting in ruku is always makruh.
These rulings emphasize proper posture-based actions in salah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن يتم القراءة في الركوع كما في منية المصلي وفي موضع آخر أن يقرأ في غير حالة القيام ومنها أن يقوم خلف الصف وحده مقتدياً بالإمام إلا إذا لم يجد فرجة، وكذا يكره للمنفرد أن يقوم في خلال الصفوف فيصلي فيخالفهم في القيام والقعود.
Among them: Completing the recitation in ruku, as in Munyat al-Musalli; and in another place, that he recites in other than the standing position. Among them: Standing behind the row alone as a follower behind the imam, unless he finds no gap. Likewise, it is disliked for a lone person to stand in the middle of the rows and pray, thus differing from them in standing and sitting.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents two disliked acts. The first is reciting Quran during ruku or in any non-standing position; proper recitation is meant for qiyam. The second is praying alone behind the row when following the imam; one should join the row if possible. If no gap exists, it is permissible. Also, a lone person should not stand between rows (e.g., to be counted alone) as this disrupts the straightness.

Key Terms
خلف الصف وحده (khalaf al-saff wahdahu): A follower praying alone behind the row, not aligned with the congregation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is it permissible to pray alone behind the row as a follower?
A) Always permissible
B) When there is no gap to join the row
C) Only in nafl prayer
D) It is never permissible

Key Takeaway
Reciting Quran in ruku and standing alone behind the row as a follower are both makruh tanzih; the latter is permissible if no gap exists.

Places Where Prayer is Disliked (Makruh)
Lists seven makruh locations with exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven places where prayer is makruh according to the Hanafi school and state the exceptions that make prayer permissible in a bathhouse and graveyard.

Core Idea
Prayer is disliked in seven specific places, but can be valid if purified and irrelevant.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq enumerates locations where prayer is considered makruh tanzihi. The dislike is due to impurity, association with devils, or disrespect. However, the Hanafi school provides practical exceptions: a bathhouse can be used if the spot is washed and free of images; a graveyard can be used if a designated prayer area is free of graves and impurity. Understanding these rulings helps you avoid invalidating your prayer or performing it in a disliked manner.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the seven locations
Commit to memory the list: camel resting places, garbage dumps, slaughterhouses, washing places, bathhouses, graveyards, and the roof of the Kaaba.
2
Understand the reasoning
The dislike arises from impurity, association with Shaytan, or disrespect. For example, camel resting places are notorious for najasah.
3
Learn the exceptions
For a bathhouse: wash a spot and ensure no images; for a graveyard: use a designated area free of graves and impurity.
4
Recognize the ruling level
The ruling is makruh tanzih (disliked but not forbidden). Prayer in these places is valid if conditions are met.

The seven places are: camel resting places, garbage dumps, slaughterhouses, washing places, bathhouses, graveyards, and the roof of the Kaaba. However, exceptions are noted: a bathhouse can be used if the specific spot is washed and free of images; a graveyard can be used if a separate prayer area is designated with no graves or impurity. These exceptions show that the dislike is due to impurity or inappropriate setting, not inherent in the location.

Imam Hurrying and Staying After Salam
Rulings on imam's conduct: disliked actions and exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two disliked acts for the imam during congregational prayer and identify the exception for staying after taslim.

Core Idea
The imam should neither rush the congregation nor linger after taslim—except for a brief supplication.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq highlights two rulings specific to the imam's conduct. The first is avoiding haste that prevents the congregation from completing sunnah prayers. The second is not staying in place after fard if a sunnah follows, except for a short du'a. These rulings emphasize consideration for the followers and following the prophetic practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first disliked act
The imam rushing the congregation away from completing the sunnah. This is disliked because it hampers the followers' ability to perform the sunnah prayers.
Rushing here means finishing the fard so quickly that followers cannot pray the sunnah after.
2
Identify the second disliked act
The imam staying in his place after taslim in a prayer that has a sunnah after it. This is disliked because it may confuse followers or make them imitate the imam.
This applies only to prayers that have a sunnah after them, e.g., Maghrib, Isha, Fajr, Zuhr.
3
Note the exception
The imam may stay briefly to recite the prescribed supplication: 'Allahumma anta al-salam wa minka al-salam tabarakta ya dhal-jalali wal-ikram.'
The exception is limited to the time needed for this du'a, as reported in the tradition.
4
Understand the rationale
These rulings ensure that the imam facilitates the congregation's worship and avoids actions that may lead to unnecessary imitation or pressure.
The underlying principle is consideration of the congregation's needs and following the Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنه يكره للإمام أن يعجلهم عن إكمال السنة ومنها ويكره أن يمكث في مكانه بعد ما سلم في صلاة بعدها سنة إلا قدر ما يقول اللهم أنت السلام ومنك السلام تباركت يا ذا الجلال والإكرام، به ورد الأثر كما في منية المصلي.
Among them: It is disliked for the imam to hurry them away from completing the sunnah. Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage discusses two disliked behaviors specific to the imam. First, he should not rush the congregation so that they cannot complete the sunnah prayers (rawatib). Second, after the fard prayer, if there is a sunnah to follow, the imam should not linger in his place except for a brief supplication. The prescribed formula is mentioned. This encourages the imam to leave quickly so that people can pray their sunnah without distraction or imitation of the imam.

Praying While Needing to Relieve Oneself and the Ranks of Mustahabb
Ruling and classification of recommended acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on praying while needing to relieve oneself, explain the varying degrees of mustahabb, and distinguish khilaf al-awla from makruh tanzihi.

Core Idea
Praying while needing to relieve oneself is valid but makruh; mustahabb acts have variable rank—some leave as makruh, others not.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq combines a practical ruling on prayer when one has an urgent need to relieve oneself with a deeper exploration of the hierarchical nature of mustahabb acts. It addresses a common misconception: that all recommended acts are equal. The commentary unpacks the relationship between khilaf al-awla and makruh tanzihi, showing that leaving a mustahabb act is not necessarily disliked unless specific evidence indicates karahah. Through the example of eating on Eid al-Adha, it illustrates how the same action can be mustahabb while its omission is not disliked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
Determine the hukm for praying while needing to relieve oneself and note the condition of being distracted. The text states that if the need distracts, one must break the prayer; if completed, it is valid but sinful (makruh tanzihi).
Also note the distinction for traveler vs. one at home regarding adhan/iqama.
2
Understand the expansion on mustahabb ranks
Recognize that mustahabb acts have varying levels: some if left become makruh tanzihi, others do not. The text explicitly says 'the ranks of mustahabb are varying like the ranks of sunnah, wajib, and fard.'
3
Analyze the relationship between khilaf al-awla and makruh tanzihi
See that khilaf al-awla is broader: leaving mustahabb is always khilaf al-awla but not always makruh tanzihi. Evidence (dalil) is needed for the latter.
The passage clarifies that makruh tanzihi requires a specific evidence; khilaf al-awla is more general.
4
Apply to the example of Eid eating
The delay of eating Adha meat until after prayer is mustahabb, but eating earlier is not makruh. This demonstrates that leaving a mustahabb does not automatically incur dislike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن يدخل في الصلاة وقد أخذه غائط أو بول وإن كان الاهتمام يشغله يقطعها وإن مضى عليها أجزأه وقد أساء، وكذا إن أخذه بعد الأذان والإقامة للمسافر ولمن يصلي في بيته في المصر وبكره تركهما للأول دون الثاني، فعلم أن مراتب الاستحباب متفاوته كمراتب السنة والواجب والفرض .اهـ. ومثله في شرح الباقاني. وحينئذ فيكون بعض المستحبات تركها مكروهاً تنزيهاً وبعضها غير مكروه ومنه الأكل يوم الأضحى فإنه لو لم يؤخره إلى ما بعد الصلاة لا يكره مع أن التأخير مستحب. والمراد نفي الكراهة أصلاً خلافاً لما قدمناه عن بعض الفضلاء لما سيأتي في باب العيد من قوله لأن الكراهة لا بد لها من دليل خاص، وسيأتي تمامه هناك شاء الله تعالى. وبذلك يندفع الإشكال لأن المكروه تنزيهاً الذي ثبتت كراهته بالدليل يكون خلاف الأولى، ولا يلزم من كون الشيء خلاف الأولى أن يكون مكروهاً تنزيهاً ما لم يوجه. دليل الكراهة والحاصل أن خلاف الأولى أعم من المكروه تنزيهاً وترك المستحب خلاف الأولى دائماً لا مكروه تنزيهاً دائماً بل قد يكون مكروهاً إن وجد دليل الكراهة وإلا فلا قوله: (وذكر في الفتاوى الخ) وقيل يكره لأنه مأوى الشياطين وبالأول يفتى. كذا في الفيض ولا بأس بالصلاة في موضع جلوس الحمامي. كذا في الخانية وهو موضع نزع الثياب المصرح به النهر كذا في شرح الشيخ إسماعيل قوله : (أعد للصلاة لأن الكراهة معللة بالتشبه بأهل الكتاب وهو منتف فيما كان على الصفة المذكورة حلبي .
Among them: Entering prayer while afflicted by stool or urine; if the preoccupation distracts him, he should cut it off; if he completes it, it suffices him and he has done wrong. Likewise, if it afflicts him after the adhan and iqama for a traveler and for one who prays at home in the city, and it is disliked to leave them for the first but not the second. So it is known that the ranks of mustahabb are varying like the ranks of sunnah, wajib, and fard. The same is in Sharh al-Baqani. Therefore, some mustahabb acts, if left, are makruh tanzihi, and some are not makruh. From this is eating on the day of Adha; if he does not delay it until after the prayer, it is not disliked, although delaying is mustahabb. The intent is to negate dislike absolutely, contrary to what we mentioned from some virtuous men, because of what will come in the chapter of Eid from his statement: 'Dislike must have a specific evidence.' And its completion will come there, God willing. By this the problem is repelled, because the makruh tanzihi whose dislike is proven by evidence becomes khilaf al-awla, but it does not follow that something being khilaf al-awla is makruh tanzihi unless evidence for dislike is directed. The conclusion is that khilaf al-awla is more general than makruh tanzihi; leaving mustahabb is always khilaf al-awla, but not always makruh tanzihi. Rather, it may be makruh if evidence of dislike exists, otherwise not. His statement: (And it is mentioned in al-Fatawa) And it is said it is disliked because it is a dwelling of devils, and the first is given fatwa. Similarly in al-Fayd. There is no harm in praying in the place where the bath attendant sits, as in al-Khaniyya, and it is the place of removing clothes, as stated in al-Nahr, as in the commentary of Sheikh Ismail. His statement: (Prepared for prayer) because the dislike is attributed to resemblance to the People of the Book, which is absent when it is as described. Halabi.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This final lesson includes two parts. The first part is the ruling on entering prayer while needing to relieve oneself: it is makruh, and if the need is distracting, one should break the prayer; if completed, it is valid but with sin. Then the text discusses the varying degrees of mustahabb: some are so strong that leaving them becomes makruh tanzihi, while others are not. The example of eating on Adha (delaying is mustahabb but not failing to delay is not makruh) illustrates this. The passage then resolves earlier objections by clarifying that khilaf al-awla (contrary to best) is broader than makruh tanzihi; leaving mustahabb is always khilaf al-awla but not always makruh tanzihi—only if specific evidence points to dislike. The text ends with a note on praying in a bathhouse spot, referring to fatwa that the spot prepared for prayer removes the resemblance to People of the Book.

Key Terms
خلاف الأولى (khilaf al-awla): Contrary to the best practice, but not necessarily disliked
مراتب الاستحباب (maratib al-istihbab): The varying ranks of recommended acts, some stronger than others
مكروه تنزيهي (makruh tanzihi): A disliked act that is not prohibited, often associated with leaving a recommended act

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The prayer is invalid
B) The prayer is valid but he has sinned
C) The prayer is valid and no sin
D) He must repeat the prayer

Key Takeaway
Praying with need is valid but makruh; mustahabb acts vary in karahah when omitted, requiring specific evidence for dislike.

Prayer While Distracted: Food and Bodily Needs
Ruling with proof: dislike and its exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying with food present or while resisting the call of nature, and explain the evidence and the exception for short prayer time.

Core Idea
Prayer with food or bodily need is valid but disliked; in a tight time, pray even with dislike.

This lesson addresses a common dilemma: should you pray when you are hungry and food is before you, or when you urgently need the toilet? The Prophet's hadith seems to forbid it, but Hanafi jurists interpret it as dislike, not prohibition. Let's examine the evidence and the important exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act being ruled upon: performing prayer while food is present or while resisting the call of nature. The ruling is makruh tanzih (disliked).
The dislike is for the state, not the prayer itself.
2
Cite the hadith and its apparent meaning
The primary evidence is a hadith narrated by Muslim: 'There is no prayer when food is present, nor when he is resisting the two filths.' The apparent negation (la salata) could imply invalidity or lack of perfection.
The hadith is ahad (single-narrator).
3
Explain the Hanafi interpretation
Hanafis interpret the negation as negation of perfect virtue (nafy al-fadilah), not validity. This is based on other evidence and the principle that ahad hadith cannot establish absolute prohibition when conflicting interests exist.
Thus the prayer is valid but disliked.
4
Note the exception for short prayer time
If the prayer time is so short that relieving oneself or eating would cause the time to pass, one must pray even in this state, because performing the obligation with dislike is better than making it up (qada).
Performance with karaha is preferred over qada.
5
Extension to passing wind
The commentator extends the ruling to include the need to pass wind (reyh), analogizing it to the two filths. So resisting gas is also disliked.
This is an analogy (qiyas) within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل فيه ما رواه مسلم عن عائشة رضي الله عنها قالت: سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول لا صلاة بحضرة طعام ولا وهو يدافعه الأخبثان (۱) وجعل الشارح مدافعة الريح كالأخبثين وأن الحديث محمول على الكراهية ونفي الفضيلة حتى لو ضاق الوقت بحيث لو اشتغل بالوضوء يفوته يصلي لأن الأداء مع الكراهية أولى من القضاء. ومنها أن كل عمل قليل لغير عذر فهو مكروه كما لو تروح على نفسه بمروحة أو كمه والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم.
And the basis for it is what Muslim narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) who said: I heard the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) say: 'There is no prayer when food is present, nor when he is resisting the two filths.' And the commentator made resisting wind (i.e., needing to pass gas) like the two filths, and that the hadith is interpreted as dislike and negation of excellence, such that if time is short so that if he busies himself with ablution the prayer will be missed, he prays because performance with dislike is better than making up. And among them is that every small action without excuse is disliked, like fanning oneself with a fan or one's sleeve. And Allah Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from a hadith. The core ruling: it is disliked (makruh) to begin prayer when food that one desires is present before him, or when he is urgently needing to relieve himself (urine or stool). The hadith's negation ('no prayer') is understood as negation of complete virtue, not invalidity; thus if one prays in such a state, the prayer is valid but lacking perfection. The commentator extends the ruling to include the need to pass wind (like the two filths). An important exception: if the prayer time is so short that performing wudu would cause the prayer to be missed, one must pray even in this state, since performing the obligation with some dislike is better than missing it entirely (qada). The passage also notes that any small unnecessary action during prayer, such as fanning oneself without excuse, is disliked.

Key Terms
الأخبثان (al-akhbathan): The two filths: urine and stool (or by extension, any need to relieve oneself).
كراهة (karahah): Dislike; an action that is not sinful but is better to avoid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) He should delay and pray later as qada.
D) The prayer is invalid no matter what.

Key Takeaway
Prayer is valid but disliked when distracted by food or bodily need; if time is short, perform the prayer despite the dislike.

Locking the Mosque Door and Teacher Exclusivity
Ruling on locking mosque door and teacher conduct

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on locking the mosque door, identify when it is permissible, and explain why a teacher cannot monopolize a spot in the mosque.

Core Idea
Locking the mosque door is disliked unless feared for property; no one owns a spot in the mosque.

This lesson examines the ruling on locking the door of a mosque, which is generally disliked because it may prevent worshippers from entering. However, necessity can make it permissible. The text also addresses the common problem of teachers claiming exclusive rights to a teaching spot in the mosque, which is condemned as ignorance and a potential major sin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Locking the mosque door: the default ruling is dislike (makruh) because it resembles preventing prayer (Quran 2:114).
2
Locate the exception
If there is fear for the mosque's property, locking becomes permissible (la ba'sa bihi). The criterion is fear of harm (khashyat al-darar).
3
Apply the criterion
In times of general security, locking remains disliked; in times of theft, it is allowed. The text says if fear exists always, then always permissible except possibly during prayer times.
4
Extend the principle to teacher conduct
No one owns a spot in the mosque; a teacher cannot prevent another from using his usual spot. The text condemns such exclusivity as great ignorance.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view: locking is disliked but permissible when needed; teachers have no special rights over mosque spots.

المسجد ا هـ . وهو أحسن من التقييد بزماننا كما في عبارة بعضهم فالمدار خشية الضرر على المسجد، فإن ثبت في زماننا في جميع الأوقات ثبت كذلك إلا في أوقات الصلاة أو لا فلا أو في بعضها ففي بعضها. كذا في فتح وتعليم علم وتعلمه وقراءة قرآن ولا يتعين مكان مخصوص لأحد حتى لو كان للمدرس موضع من المسجد يدرس فيه فسبقه غيره إليه ليس له إزعاجه وإقامته منه فقد قال الإمام الزاهدي في فتاويه المسماة بالقنية معزياً إلى فتاوي العصر له في المسجد موضع معين يواظب عليه وقد شغله غيره قال الأوزاعي له أن يزعجه وليس له ذلك عندنا ا هـ. ومن الفروع الدالة على أن مدرس المسجد كغيره ما قاله في القنية :أيضاً ليس للمدرس في المسجد أن يجعل من بيته باباً إلى المسجد وإن فعل أدى ضمان نقصان الجدار إن وقع فيه ا هـ. ذلك أن بعض مدرسي من أنه مدرسة وليس الأروام يعتقد في المسجد الذي له مدرس بمسجد حتى ينتهك حرمته بالمشي فيه بنعله المتنجس مع تصريح الواقف بجعله مسجداً، وسيأتي شروط المسجد إن شاء الله تعالى في كتاب الوقف. قوله : (والوطء فوقه والبول والتخلي أي وكره الوطء فوق المسجد وكذا البول والتغوط لأن سطح المسجد له حكم المسجد حتى يصح الاقتداء منه بمن تحته، ولا يبطل الاعتكاف بالصعود إليه ولا يحل للجنب الوقوف عليه. والمراد بالكراهة كراهة التحريم وصرح الشارح بأن الوطء فيه حرام لقوله تعالى ولا تباشروهن وأنتم عاكفون في المساجد ﴾ [البقرة: ١٨٧] وذكر في فتح القدير أن الحق أنها كراهة تحريم لأن الآية ظنية الدلالة لأنها محتملة كون التحريم للاعتكاف أو للمسجد وبمثلها لا يثبت التحريم ولأن تطهيره واجب لقوله تعالى وإن طهرا بيتي للطائفين والعاكفين والركع السجود) [البقرة: ١٢٥] ولما أخرجه المنذري مرفوعاً «جنبوا مساجدكم صبيانكم ومجانينكم وبيعكم وشراءكم ورفع أصواتكم وسل سيوفكم وإقامة حدودكم وجمروها في الجمع واجعلوا على أبوابها المطاهر» (۱) اهـ . واختلف المشايخ في كراهية إخراج
His statement: 'And locking the door of the mosque' because it resembles preventing prayer. Allah said: 'And who is more unjust than he who prevents the mosques of Allah from His name being mentioned therein.' [2:114] And locking resembles preventing, so it is disliked. It was said in al-Hidayah: 'And it was said: There is no harm if one fears for the property of the mosque.' End. That is better than restricting it to our time as in the expression of some, so the criterion is fear of harm to the mosque. If it is established in our time in all times, it is established likewise except in prayer times or not, or in some times then in some. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-'Inayah: The management of locking is for the people of the neighborhood, for if they agree upon a man and make him a custodian without the judge's order, he becomes a custodian. End. And in al-Nihayah: The early scholars used to dislike binding copies of the Quran and making covers for them so that it doesn't take the form of preventing Quran recitation; this is similar or even greater because the mushaf is owned by its owner but the mosque is not owned by anyone. End. I even heard from some that he attributes it to himself and says 'this is my school' or 'do not teach in it.' All this is great ignorance and may not be far from a major sin. Allah said: 'And the mosques are for Allah' [72:18] and what we recited from the previous verse. His statement: 'And treading upon it, and urinating and defecating' meaning: It is disliked to tread upon the mosque roof, and likewise urinating and defecating, because the roof of the mosque has the same ruling as the mosque, so that following (in prayer) is valid from it to the person below, i'tikaf is not invalidated by climbing to it, and it is not permissible for a junub to stand on it. The intended meaning of 'dislike' is prohibitively disliked, and the commentator explicitly stated that treading (for intercourse) therein is haram due to the verse: 'And do not approach them while you are in i'tikaf in the mosques.' [2:125] And due to what al-Mundhiri narrated as marfu': 'Keep your mosques away from your children, your insane, your buying and selling, your loud voices, your drawing of swords, and your establishment of hadd punishments, and perfume them on Fridays, and place washing places at their doors.' End. And the scholars disagreed about the dislike of removing...
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage covers two major issues: (1) locking the mosque door, and (2) treading on the mosque roof. Locking the door is generally disliked because it resembles preventing people from prayer, based on Quran 2:114. However, if there is a fear for the mosque's property, it is permissible to lock it. The contemporary scholars discuss that in our times, if theft is common, locking may be allowed. The text then goes into a lengthy discussion about teachers who monopolize spots in the mosque, claiming 'this is my school' – this is condemned as great ignorance, and it is not permissible to prevent anyone from worshipping or teaching. (2) Treading on the mosque roof (or having intercourse there) is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi). The roof shares the ruling of the mosque: following in prayer from roof to floor is valid, i'tikaf is not broken by going to the roof, and a junub cannot stand on it. The verse about i'tikaf and purification of the mosque are cited, along with a hadith about keeping mosques free from certain things. There is also mention of disagreement among scholars about the dislike of removing something (the text cuts off).

Key Terms
غلق باب المسجد (ghalqu bab al-masjid): Locking the mosque door, which is disliked as it may prevent people from entering for prayer.
مدرس المسجد (mudarris al-masjid): A teacher in the mosque, who has no exclusive right to any spot.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He may remove him because the spot is his by right.
B) It is permissible if the first teacher arrived earlier.
C) The teacher has no right to prevent or remove the other teacher.
D) The mosque administration can decide.

Key Takeaway
Locking the mosque is disliked but permissible when necessary; no one owns a spot in the mosque.

Ruling on Treading on the Mosque Roof
Ruling on treading on mosque roof and its evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on treading (including sexual intercourse) on the mosque roof, identify the evidence, and explain why it is prohibitively disliked rather than haram.

Core Idea
Treading on the mosque roof is prohibitively disliked because the evidence is speculative.

This lesson covers the ruling on using the mosque roof, whether for walking or for intercourse. The Hanafi school holds that it is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) due to the roof sharing the mosque's sanctity. The evidence from the Quran is considered zanni al-dalala, so it does not reach the level of haram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Treading on the mosque roof, as well as urinating and defecating, is disliked (karahah). The text specifies that the intended meaning is prohibitively disliked (karahah tahrimiyyah).
2
Examine the basis for the ruling
The roof shares the same ruling as the mosque: valid following in prayer, i'tikaf not broken by climbing, and a person in janabah may not stand on it.
3
Analyze the Quranic evidence
The verse 'Do not approach them while you are in i'tikaf in the mosques' (2:187) is cited. The commentator in Fath al-Qadir notes that this verse is zanni al-dalala (speculative in evidence) because it could be interpreted as relating to i'tikaf rather than the mosque itself.
4
Determine the legal consequence
Because the evidence is speculative, prohibition (tahrim) is not established. Hence the ruling is karahah tahrimiyyah, not haram. The hadith about purifying the mosque confirms the obligation to keep it pure.
5
Apply the ruling
For worldly purposes, treading on the roof is makruh tahrimi. For intercourse specifically, some scholars say haram, but the relied-upon view is also makruh tahrimi due to the speculative evidence.

Source Text

قوله : وغلق باب المسجد لأنه يشبه المنع من الصلاة قال تعالى ومن أظلم ممن منع مساجد الله أن يذكر فيها اسمه [البقرة : ١١٤] والإغلاق يشبه المنع فيكره قال في الهداية : وقيل لا بأس به إذا خيف على متاع المسجد ا هـ . وهو أحسن من التقييد بزماننا كما في عبارة بعضهم فالمدار خشية الضرر على المسجد، فإن ثبت في زماننا في جميع الأوقات ثبت كذلك إلا في أوقات الصلاة أو لا فلا أو في بعضها ففي بعضها. كذا في فتح القدير. وفي العناية : والتدبير في الغلق لأهل المحلة فإنهم إذا اجتمعوا على رجل وجعلوه متولياً بغير أمر القاضي يكون متوليا ا هـ. وفي النهاية وكان المتقدمون يكرهون شد المصاحف واتخاذ المشدة لها كيلا يكون ذلك في صورة المنع من قراءة القرآن، فهذا مثله أو فوقه لأن المصحف ملك لصاحبه والمسجد ليس بملك لأحد ا.هـ. ومن هنا يعلم جهل بعض مدرسي زماننا من منعهم من يدرس في مسجد تقرر في تدريسه أو كراهتهم لذلك زاعمين الاختصاص بها دون غيرهم حتى سمعت من بعضهم أنه يضيفها إلى نفسه ويقول هذه مدرستي أو لا تدرس في وأعجب من ذلك أنه إذا غضب على شخص يمنعه من دخول المسجد خصوصاً أمر دنيوي وهذا كله جهل عظيم ولا يبعد أن يكون Hierarchy of Mosques and Rulings on I'tikaf
Ranking mosques from most to least virtuous and explaining rules for i'tikaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the hierarchy of mosques by sanctity and identify the conditions for valid i'tikaf in each type.

Core Idea
Mosques differ in sanctity; i'tikaf is only valid for men in mosques with an imam and mu'adhdhin.

Not all mosques are equal in Islamic law. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes a clear hierarchy from Masjid al-Haram down to home prayer rooms, and explains how this hierarchy affects the validity of i'tikaf. Understanding this ranking helps you apply the correct rulings when choosing a mosque for worship or seclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the full hierarchy
List the six categories of mosques in order from most to least sacred as given in the text.
The order is: Masjid al-Haram → Prophet's Mosque → Masjid al-Aqsa → congregational mosques → neighborhood mosques → street mosques → home mosques.
2
Distinguish the two categories of lesser mosques
Note that neighborhood and street mosques (masajid al-mahall wa al-shawari') are grouped together as 'lower rank' (akhaff martabah). Home mosques (masajid al-buyut) are treated separately.
The text says ثم مساجد المحال ثم مساجد الشوارع, then separately ثم مساجد البيوت.
3
Apply the i'tikaf condition for men
The text rules that i'tikaf is not permitted in neighborhood or street mosques unless they have a known imam and mu'adhdhin. Understand that this condition makes them equivalent to congregational mosques for i'tikaf purposes.
The phrase إذا لم يكن لها إمام معلوم ومؤذن sets the condition.
4
Recognize the exception for women
Home mosques (private prayer rooms) are not valid for men's i'tikaf but are permitted for women. Note the gender-based distinction.
The text explicitly says لا يجوز الاعتكاف فيها إلا للنساء.
5
Synthesize the ruling pattern
Overall, the higher the sanctity of the mosque, the fewer restrictions on i'tikaf. The rulings become more restrictive as the mosque loses its public, congregational character.
This reflects the Hanafi principle that i'tikaf requires a mosque where congregational prayers are regularly held (masjid jama'ah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعظم المساجد حرمة المسجد الحرام ثم مسجد المدينة ثم مسجد بيت المقدس ثم الجوامع ثم مساجد المحال ثم مساجد الشوارع فإنها أخف مرتبة حتى لا يعتكف فيها أحد إذا لم يكن لها إمام معلوم ومؤذن ، ثم مساجد البيوت فإنه لا يجوز الاعتكاف فيها إلا للنساء.
The greatest of mosques in sanctity is the Sacred Mosque (Masjid al-Haram), then the Mosque of Madinah, then the Mosque of Jerusalem (Bayt al-Maqdis), then the congregational mosques (jawami'), then the mosques of neighborhoods (masajid al-mahall), then the mosques of streets (masajid al-shawari'). They are of a lower rank, so that no one may observe i'tikaf in them if they do not have a known imam and a mu'adhdhin. Then the mosques of houses (masajid al-buyut) – it is not permissible to observe i'tikaf in them except for women.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage ranks mosques by sanctity (hurmah). The most sacred is Masjid al-Haram in Makkah, then the Prophet's Mosque in Madinah, then al-Aqsa in Jerusalem, then Friday mosques (jami'), then neighborhood mosques, then street mosques. The lesser mosques (street and neighborhood) have lower rank; for example, i'tikaf (seclusion) is not valid in them unless they have a designated imam and mu'adhdhin. Finally, mosque-like prayer rooms in homes (masajid al-buyut) do not count as public mosques; only women may perform i'tikaf there. The sequence establishes a hierarchy for rulings like entering, greeting, and i'tikaf.

Key Terms
المسجد الحرام (al-masjid al-haram): The Sacred Mosque in Makkah, the most sanctified mosque in Islam.
الجامع (al-jami'): A congregational mosque where Friday (Jumu'ah) prayers are held.
الاعتكاف (al-i'tikaf): Seclusion in a mosque for worship, typically during Ramadan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, in which type of mosque is i'tikaf not permissible for men if the mosque lacks an imam and mu'adhdhin?
A) The Prophet's Mosque
B) Neighborhood mosques and street mosques
C) Masjid al-Haram
D) Mosques inside homes

Key Takeaway
Mosques have a hierarchy of sanctity, and i'tikaf for men is only valid in mosques with a known imam and mu'adhdhin; women may perform i'tikaf in home mosques.

Dividing a Mosque and Governing Authority
Ruling on physical division and authority over mosques

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dividing a mosque, identify which authority controls such decisions, and distinguish between permissible and impermissible uses of mosque premises.

Core Idea
Residents may divide a neighborhood mosque with a wall; only the ruler may divide a Friday mosque.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses two scenarios: how residents can physically partition their local mosque and who has authority over a Friday mosque (jami'). It also clarifies that teaching in the mosque is not its intended purpose, though a difference exists between Abu Hanifah and his two companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its permissibility
The passage states that if residents divide the mosque by building a wall, each section having its own imam, it is permissible (la ba'sa bihi). This is the basic ruling.
The permissibility applies only if the residents agree and a physical barrier is erected.
2
Note the condition: agreement and wall
The text conditionally permits division 'if they build a wall'. Thus, a mere conceptual division is insufficient; a physical partition is required.
This ensures that the two sections are distinct prayer areas.
3
Distinguish neighborhood mosque from Friday mosque (jami')
For a neighborhood mosque, residents make decisions. For a Friday mosque (jami'), the ruler (imam) or his deputy controls management, maintenance, and division. The ruler may turn a jami' into two mosques by building a wall.
This distinction is based on the public status of the jami'.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi disagreement on teaching
The passage notes that Abu Hanifah prohibits teaching in a shop located in the mosque's courtyard. His two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) permit it if no harm to the public. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the permissibility view.
The disagreement shows a broader principle: the mosque's primary purpose is prayer; other activities require careful evaluation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قسم أهل المحلة المسجد وضربوا فيه حائطاً ولكل منهم إمام على حدة ومؤذنهم واحد لا بأس به، والأولى أن يكون لكل طائفة مؤذن كما يجوز لأهل المحلة أن يجعلوا المسجد الواحد مسجدين فلهم أن يجعلوا المسجدين واحداً لإقامة الجماعات إما للتدريس أو للتذكير فلا، لأنه ما بني له وإن جاز ولا يجوز التعليم في دكان في فناء المسجد عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما يجوز إذا لم يضر بالعامة ا هـ . ما في القنية. ولا يخفى أن المسجد الجامع تدبيره وعمارته وإصلاحه للإمام أو نائبه كما صرحوا به في كتاب القسامة فللإمام أو نائبه أن يجعل الجامع مسجدين بضرب حائط ونحوه كالأهل المحلة .
If the residents of a neighborhood divide the mosque and build a wall in it, each having a separate imam while their mu'adhdhin is one, there is no harm. It is better that each group has its own mu'adhdhin. Similarly, it is permissible for the residents to make one mosque into two mosques; likewise they may make two mosques into one for establishing congregations. As for teaching or giving reminders (tadhkir), it is not allowed, because it was not built for that, even if it is permissible (in some other sense). Teaching in a shop in the courtyard of the mosque is not permissible according to Abu Hanifah; according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is permissible if it does not harm the public. End quote from al-Qunyah. It is well known that the management, maintenance, and repair of the Friday mosque (jami') are the responsibility of the ruler (imam) or his deputy, as they explained in the Book of Qasamah. Thus the ruler or his deputy may turn the Friday mosque into two mosques by building a wall, etc., similar to the residents of a neighborhood.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage addresses the division of mosques. Residents of a neighborhood may partition a single mosque with a wall, each section having a separate imam but sharing a mu'adhdhin (though it is preferred that each has its own). Similarly, they can convert one mosque into two or two into one for practical purposes (e.g., to hold multiple congregations). However, using the mosque for teaching or preaching (tadhkir) is not allowed because it was not built for that, though some exceptions exist. Teaching in a shop within the mosque's courtyard is prohibited by Abu Hanifah but permitted by the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) as long as no public nuisance occurs. Finally, for a Friday mosque (jami'), the ultimate authority is the ruler (imam) or his deputy; they can decide to partition the jami' if needed.

Key Terms
قسمة المسجد (qismat al-masjid): The act of dividing a mosque into two or more parts, physically or administratively.
الإمام (al-imam): Here: the leader of prayer in the mosque; also refers to the ruler (political leader) in the latter part.
المؤذن (al-mu'adhdhin): The person who calls the adhan for prayers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, teaching in a shop located in the mosque's courtyard is:
A) Absolutely prohibited
B) Permissible only with the ruler's permission
C) Permissible if it does not harm the public
D) Recommended

Key Takeaway
Residents may divide a neighborhood mosque with a wall, but teaching in its premises is restricted; the ruler controls the Friday mosque.
