The Ruling and Performance of Tahiyyat al-Masjid
Ruling and conditions for tahiyyat al-masjid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tahiyyat al-masjid (sunnah muakkadah), identify when it is disliked, and explain that it is not waived by sitting.

Core Idea
Tahiyyat al-masjid is a confirmed sunnah, disliked during makruh times, and not waived by sitting.

When you enter the mosque, you are entering the house of Allah. The greeting prayer is an act of devotion to Him. The Hanafi school considers it a confirmed sunnah, but with important nuances: it is disliked during prohibited times, and if you sit down, you have not missed it—you can pray later. Let's explore the rulings.

Guided Path
1
Define the act
Understand that tahiyyat al-masjid means greeting Allah, the Lord of the mosque, not the building. It is a prayer of two rak'ahs.
Al-Halabi explained this.
2
State the ruling
The Hanafi school holds it is sunnah muakkadah based on hadith and consensus.
zahir_riwayah
3
Identify exceptions
It is disliked during the prohibited times (makruh awqat). Also, if one enters multiple times, two rak'ahs per day suffice.
Preference given to general prohibition.
4
Explain the non-waiver by sitting
Sitting does not waive it; one can pray later, as proven by the hadith of Abu Dharr.
Majority view is to pray immediately, but delayed is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بد أن نذكر أحكام تحية المسجد فنقول : هي على حذف مضاف أي تحية رب المسجد لأن المقصود منها التقرب إلى الله تعالى لا إلى المسجد لأن الإنسان إذا دخل بيت الملك فإنما يحيي الملك لا بيته . كذا ذكره العلامة الحلبي . وقد حكى الإجماع على سنيتها غير أن أصحابنا يكرهونها في الأوقات المكروهة تقديماً لعموم الحاظر على عموم المبيح، وقد قدمنا أنه إذا تكرر دخوله في كل يوم فإنه يكفيه ركعتان لها في اليوم. وذكره في الغاية أنها لا تسقط بالجلوس عند أصحابنا فإنه قال في الحاكم إذا دخل المسجد للحاكم فهو بالخيار عندنا إن شاء صلى تحية المسجد عند دخوله وإن شاء صلاها عند انصرافه فلم تسقط بالجلوس لأنها لتعظيم المسجد وحرمته ففي أي وقت صلاها حصل وفي الظهيرية : ثم اختلفوا في صلاة التحية أنه يجلس ثم يقوم ويصلي أو يصلي قبل أن يجلس . قال بعضهم يجلس ثم يقوم. وعامة العلماء قالوا: يصلي كما يدخل المسجدا هـ. قلت ويشهد لقول العامة وهو الصحيح كما في القنية ما في الصحيحين عن أبي قتادة الأنصاري قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا دخل أحدكم المسجد فلا يجلس حتى يصلي ركعتين (۱) وإنما قلنا بعدم سقوطها بالجلوس لما أخرجه ابن حبان في صحيحه عن أبي ذر قال : دخلت المسجد فإذا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم جالس وحده فقال : يا أبا ذر إن للمسجد تحية وإن تحيته ركعتان فقم فاركعهما فقمت فركعتهما ا هـ. وقد قالوا: إن كل صلاة صلاها عند دخوله فرضاً أو سنة فإنها تقوم مقام التحية بلا نية كما في البدائع وغيره، فلو نوى التحية الفرض فظاهر ما في المحيط وغيره أنه وعند محمد لا يكون في الصلاة فإنهم قالوا لو نوى الدخول في الظهر والتطوع فإنه يجوز عن الفرض عند أبي يوسف وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة، وعند محمد لا يكون داخلاً.
We must mention the rulings of the greeting of the mosque (tahiyyat al-masjid). We say: It is based on an omitted genitive, meaning 'greeting of the Lord of the mosque', because the intended purpose is to draw near to Allah, not to the mosque. When a person enters the king's house, he greets the king, not the house. This was mentioned by 'Allamah al-Halabi. Consensus (ijma') has been reported on its being sunnah, but our companions dislike it during the disliked times, giving precedence to the general prohibition over the general permissibility. We have previously stated that if he enters repeatedly every day, two rak'ahs per day suffice for it. Al-Ghayah mentions that it is not waived by sitting according to our companions. Al-Hakim said: If one enters the mosque for the judge (al-hakim), he has a choice according to us: if he wishes, he may pray the greeting upon entering, or upon leaving; it is not waived by sitting because it is for the glorification of the mosque and its sanctity; whenever he prays it, it is achieved. In al-Zahiriyyah: They differed about the greeting prayer: should he sit then stand and pray, or pray before sitting? Some said he sits then stands. The majority of scholars said: pray as he enters the mosque. End quote. I say, supporting the majority view, which is the correct one as in al-Qunyah, is what is in the two Sahihs from Abu Qatadah al-Ansari: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'If one of you enters the mosque, do not sit until he prays two rak'ahs.' We only ruled that it is not waived by sitting because of what Ibn Hibban narrated in his Sahih from Abu Dharr: I entered the mosque and the Messenger of Allah was sitting alone; he said: 'O Abu Dharr, the mosque has a greeting, and its greeting is two rak'ahs; so stand and pray them.' So I stood and prayed them. End quote. They said: Any prayer that one prays upon entering, whether obligatory (fard) or sunnah, stands in place of the greeting without intention, as in al-Bada'i' and others. If one intended the greeting for the obligatory (fard), the apparent ruling in al-Muhit and others is that it is valid according to Abu Yusuf, which is a narration from Abu Hanifah, but according to Muhammad it is not valid.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage comprehensively discusses the prayer of greeting the mosque (tahiyyat al-masjid). It first explains the meaning: it is actually 'greeting of the Lord of the mosque' (tahiyyat Rabb al-masjid), because the greeting is directed to Allah, not the building. There is consensus that it is a sunnah. However, Hanafis dislike it during the prohibited times (makruh times) due to the general prohibition. If one enters multiple times a day, two rak'ahs per day suffice for all entries. Importantly, the greeting is not waived by sitting—one can pray it later, even after leaving. The majority view (supported by hadith from Abu Qatadah) is to pray immediately upon entering, though some said to sit first. A hadith from Abu Dharr (narrated by Ibn Hibban) proves that the greeting can be done even after sitting, as the Prophet told him to stand and pray when he was already sitting. Thus, the greeting is not waived by sitting. Moreover, any prayer (fard or nafl) prayed upon entering suffices for the greeting without a specific intention (niyyah) for tahiyyah. However, if one intends the greeting for the fard prayer, there is a dispute: Abu Yusuf (and one narration from Abu Hanifah) says it is valid, but Muhammad says it is not—the prayer becomes solely fard.

Key Terms
تحية المسجد (tahiyyat al-masjid): Two rak'ahs of prayer performed upon entering a mosque as a greeting to Allah, the Lord of the mosque.
الأوقات المكروهة (al-awqat al-makruhah): Times when prayer is disliked: after Fajr until sunrise, after Asr until sunset, and during the zenith of the sun.
الجلوس (al-julus): Sitting down in the mosque after entering; the text discusses whether it waives the obligation of tahiyyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person enters the mosque and sits down without praying tahiyyat al-masjid, what is the ruling?
A) The sunnah is lost and cannot be recovered
B) He may still pray it later, even after sitting
C) He must pray it while sitting
D) He is sinful for sitting before praying

Key Takeaway
Tahiyyat al-masjid is a flexible sunnah: it can be prayed immediately or later, but avoid makruh times.

The Intention for Tahiyyat al-Masjid: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on intention for tahiyyat al-masjid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on intending tahiyyat for fard, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Intending the greeting as a fard prayer is valid per Abu Yusuf and one narration from Abu Hanifah, but invalid per Muhammad.

The Hanafi school allows any prayer upon entering to substitute for tahiyyat al-masjid. But what if you specifically intend your fard prayer as the greeting? The Sahibayn differed. Let's examine their proofs and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Any prayer (fard or sunnah) prayed upon entering suffices for tahiyyah without a specific intention.
As in al-Bada'i'.
2
Present the dispute
If one explicitly intends the tahiyyah for the fard (e.g., intending the fard as the greeting), Abu Yusuf validates it; Muhammad invalidates it.
Also narration from Abu Hanifah.
3
Analyze the basis
Abu Yusuf views the intention as merging two acts; Muhammad sees them as separate and the greeting cannot substitute for fard.
Root difference in niyyah rulings.
4
State the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) is the majority view that any prayer suffices without specific intention. The validity of merging is a weaker opinion.
Based on al-Muhit and others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Disliked Acts in Mosques: Talking, Sleeping, and Condolence
Rulings on worldly speech, sleeping, and condolence in mosques.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on talking, sleeping, and condolence gatherings in mosques, and identify the intra-Hanafi difference regarding sleeping and condolence.

Core Idea
Worldly talk, sleeping, and condolence sittings in mosques are disliked; the mosque is built for prayer, not worldly gatherings.

This passage from the Hanafi fiqh discusses three actions that are generally considered disliked (makruh) in mosques: engaging in worldly conversation, sleeping, and holding condolence gatherings. The author cites different authorities and notes scholarly differences, particularly regarding sleeping and condolence. Understanding these rulings helps maintain the sanctity of the mosque while recognizing legitimate exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three disliked acts and their general ruling
List the acts: talking (makruh, but conditioned on sitting for that purpose), sleeping (disliked per majority, but some permit), condolence (disliked per majority, but Abu al-Layth permits based on prophetic precedent).
The general ruling is makruh tanzihi for all three.
2
Understand the condition for talking
The dislike applies only if one sits specifically to talk; if one sits for worship then talks afterwards, it is not disliked. This is from al-Zahiriyyah and Fath al-Qadir.
Intent and context matter.
3
Distinguish the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position is to dislike sleeping and condolence, despite the permissive views of some scholars. The Prophet's action for condolence is considered specific to that situation.
The majority view prevails in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية بكراهة الحديث أي كلام الناس في المسجد لكن قيده بأن يجلس لأجله. وفي فتح القدير : الكلام المباح فيه مكروه يأكل الحسنات، وينبغي تقييده بما في الظهيرية. أما إن جلس للعبادة ثم بعدها تكلم فلا وأما النوم في المسجد فاختلف المشايخ فيه. وفي التجنيس : الأشبه بما تقدم من المسائل أنه يكره لأنه ما أعد لذلك وإنما بني لإقامة الصلاة، وأما الجلوس في المسجد للمصيبة فمكروه لأنه لم يبن له وعن الفقيه أبي الليث أنه لا بأس به لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حين بلغه قتل جعفر وزيد بن حارثة جلس في المسجد والناس يأتونه ويعزونه، والمفتي به أنه قوله : (قيده بأن يجلس لأجله قال في النهر والإطلاق أوجه قوله : (وصحح في مصلي العيد
In al-Zahiriyyah: It is disliked (makruh) to talk, i.e., people's speech in the mosque, but he conditioned it with sitting for the sake of it. In Fath al-Qadir: Permissible speech in it is disliked, it eats away good deeds, and it should be restricted by what is in al-Zahiriyyah. As for if he sits for worship then afterwards speaks, no (dislike). As for sleeping in the mosque, the mashayikh differed about it. In al-Tajnis: The more similar to the preceding issues is that it is disliked because it was not prepared for that; it was only built for establishing prayer. As for sitting in the mosque for a calamity (mourning), it is disliked because it was not built for it. According to al-Faqih Abu al-Layth, there is no harm in it because the Prophet, peace be upon him, when he was informed of the martyrdom of Ja'far and Zayd ibn Harithah, sat in the mosque and people came to him to offer condolences. The fatwa is according to his statement: (Qayyadah bi an yajlis li-ajlihi) said in al-Nahr: al-Itlaq awjah. His statement: (wa sahha fi musalli al-'id)
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage continues the theme of disliked actions in mosques. It quotes al-Zahiriyyah: talking (worldly conversation) in the mosque is disliked, but only if one sits specifically for that purpose (if conversation arises after worship, it is not disliked). Fath al-Qadir adds that even permissible talk is disliked and reduces rewards. Sleeping in the mosque is a point of difference among scholars; the preferred view (as in al-Tajnis) is that it is disliked because the mosque is not built for that. Sitting for condolence (after a death) is also disliked, but al-Faqih Abu al-Layth permits it based on the Prophet's example during the mourning for Ja'far and Zayd. The mufta bihi position is to dislike it, unless one sits specifically for it? The text ends abruptly, but the gist is that worldly talk, sleeping, and condolence gatherings are all disliked in mosques.

Key Terms
الكلام المباح (al-kalam al-mubah): Permissible worldly speech that is not sinful; the text rules it is disliked in mosques.
الجلوس للمصيبة (al-julus lil-musibah): Sitting in the mosque to receive condolences after a death; considered disliked because mosques are not for mourning gatherings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Recommended (mustahabb)
B) Disliked (makruh)

D) Permissible and allowed

Key Takeaway
Worldly talk, sleeping, and condolence sittings in mosques are disliked; the essential principle is that a mosque is built for prayer, not worldly or mourning gatherings.

Tahiyyat al-Masjid: Ruling, Proofs, and Practical Application
Explains the ruling of tahiyyat al-masjid with proofs and substitution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of tahiyyat al-masjid, identify its proof, and apply the rules regarding timing, sitting, and substitution by other prayers.

Core Idea
Tahiyyat al-masjid is a confirmed sunnah but yields to the prohibition of prayer at disliked times.

When you enter a mosque, there is a recommended greeting: two rak'ahs of prayer. But what if you enter during a prohibited time? What if you sit down first? And can another prayer suffice? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on tahiyyat al-masjid, its proof, and the practical nuances that every Muslim should know.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that tahiyyat al-masjid is a two-rak'ah sunnah prayer prescribed upon entering a mosque. Note its purpose: greeting Allah, not the building.
The ruling is sunnah muakkadah (confirmed).
2
Examine the proof
Consider the hadith evidence: the command to pray before sitting (Bukhari/Muslim) and the incident of Abu Dharr (Ibn Hibban), which shows the greeting is not invalidated by sitting.
The Prophet (peace be upon him) explicitly instructed prayer upon entry.
3
Apply the exception for disliked times
Understand that the Hanafi school gives precedence to the general prohibition of prayer during the five makruh times, so tahiyyat al-masjid is avoided then.
This is a case of ta'arud (conflict) between two general rulings.
4
Understand substitution and intention
Any prayer (fard or nafl) performed upon entry suffices for the greeting, without the need for a specific intention. Note the intra-Hanafi difference regarding intention for fard.
Abu Yusuf allows it; Muhammad al-Shaybani does not.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The preferred practice is to pray before sitting, as held by the majority of scholars and supported by the hadith. If one sits, the greeting remains valid but the sunnah is lost.
The author cites al-Qunyah for the correct view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسجدين بضرب حائط ونحوه كالأهل المحلة. ولا بد أن نذكر أحكام تحية المسجد فنقول : هي على حذف مضاف أي تحية رب المسجد لأن المقصود منها التقرب إلى الله تعالى لا إلى المسجد لأن الإنسان إذا دخل بيت الملك فإنما يحيي الملك لا بيته . كذا ذكره العلامة الحلبي . وقد حكى الإجماع على سنيتها غير أن أصحابنا يكرهونها في الأوقات المكروهة تقديماً لعموم الحاظر على عموم المبيح، وقد قدمنا أنه إذا تكرر دخوله في كل يوم فإنه يكفيه ركعتان لها في اليوم. وذكره في الغاية أنها لا تسقط بالجلوس عند أصحابنا فإنه قال في الحاكم إذا دخل المسجد للحاكم فهو بالخيار عندنا إن شاء صلى تحية المسجد عند دخوله وإن شاء صلاها عند انصرافه فلم تسقط بالجلوس لأنها لتعظيم المسجد وحرمته ففي أي وقت صلاها حصل وفي الظهيرية : ثم اختلفوا في صلاة التحية أنه يجلس ثم يقوم ويصلي أو يصلي قبل أن يجلس . قال بعضهم يجلس ثم يقوم. وعامة العلماء قالوا: يصلي كما يدخل المسجد اهـ. قلت ويشهد لقول العامة وهو الصحيح كما في القنية ما في الصحيحين عن أبي قتادة الأنصاري قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا دخل أحدكم المسجد فلا يجلس حتى يصلي ركعتين (۱) وإنما قلنا بعدم سقوطها بالجلوس لما أخرجه ابن حبان في صحيحه عن أبي ذر قال : دخلت المسجد فإذا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم جالس وحده فقال : يا أبا ذر إن للمسجد تحية وإن تحيته ركعتان فقم فاركعهما فقمت فركعتهما اهـ. وقد قالوا: إن كل صلاة صلاها عند دخوله فرضاً أو سنة فإنها تقوم مقام التحية بلا نية كما في البدائع وغيره، فلو نوى التحية الفرض فظاهر ما في المحيط وغيره أنه عند أبي يوسف وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة، وعند محمد لا يكون داخلاً.
Two mosques by striking a wall and the like, like the people of the neighborhood. And we must mention the rulings of greeting the mosque, so we say: It is on ellipsis of the possesed [noun], i.e., greeting the Lord of the mosque because the purpose of it is to draw close to Allah Almighty, not to the mosque, for when a person enters the king's house, he greets the king, not his house. Thus mentioned by 'Allamah al-Halabi. And consensus on its being sunna has been reported, except that our companions (Hanafis) consider it disliked in the disliked times, giving precedence to the general prohibition over the general permission. And we have already mentioned that if his entry is repeated every day, two rak'ahs for it in the day suffice him. And it is mentioned in al-Ghayah that it is not cancelled by sitting according to our companions, for he said in al-Hakim: When the judge enters the mosque, he has a choice according to us: if he wishes, he prays the greeting of the mosque upon his entry, and if he wishes, he prays it at his departure. So it is not cancelled by sitting because it is for the veneration and sanctity of the mosque, so no matter when he prays it, it is attained. And in al-Zahiriyyah: Then they differed regarding the greeting prayer: whether he sits then stands and prays, or prays before sitting. Some said he sits then stands. The general body of scholars said: he prays as he enters the mosque. I say: And what supports the statement of the general body, which is the correct one as in al-Qunyah, is what is in the two Sahihs from Abu Qatadah al-Ansari who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: When one of you enters the mosque, let him not sit until he prays two rak'ahs. And we only say it is not cancelled by sitting because of what Ibn Hibban narrated in his Sahih from Abu Dharr who said: I entered the mosque and there was the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) sitting alone, so he said: O Abu Dharr, indeed the mosque has a greeting, and its greeting is two rak'ahs, so stand and pray them. So I stood and prayed them. And they have said: Indeed every prayer that one prays upon his entry, whether obligatory or sunna, stands in the place of the greeting without intention, as in al-Bada'i' and elsewhere. So if he intends the greeting with the obligatory prayer, the apparent (view) in al-Muhit and elsewhere is that according to Abu Yusuf, and it is a narration from Abu Hanifah, it is valid, and according to Muhammad, he does not enter (the prayer).
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a detailed examination of the ruling of tahiyyat al-masjid (greeting the mosque). The author begins by clarifying that the greeting is actually for Allah, not the building, and then notes consensus that it is a sunnah. However, the Hanafi school dislikes it during the prohibited times (makruh) because prohibitions take precedence over permissions. If one enters the mosque multiple times daily, two rak'ahs per day suffice. Importantly, the greeting is not nullified by sitting; if one sits without praying, he may still pray later since the purpose is reverence. The author then addresses the timing: should one pray immediately upon entering or may he sit first? The majority opinion, supported by a hadith from Abu Qatadah in Bukhari and Muslim, is to pray before sitting. The author also cites a hadith from Abu Dharr where the Prophet commanded him to pray after sitting, proving the greeting remains valid even if offered later. Lastly, the text states that any prayer—fard or nafl—performed upon entering suffices for the greeting without a specific intention. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: according to Abu Yusuf (and a narration from Abu Hanifah), if one intends the greeting with a fard prayer, it is valid; Muhammad al-Shaybani holds it is not (he does not enter the prayer with that intention). The relied-upon view is that one should pray before sitting.

Key Terms
تحية المسجد (tahiyyat al-masjid): A two-rak'ah prayer offered upon entering a mosque as a greeting to Allah, not to the building itself.
الأوقات المكروهة (al-awqat al-makruhah): The five times when it is disliked to pray: at sunrise, at noon (when the sun is at its zenith), at sunset, and after Fajr and 'Asr prayers.
قيام مقام التحية (qiyam maqam al-tahiyyah): Any prayer (fard or sunnah) offered upon entering the mosque suffices as the greeting, without the need for a specific intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a person enters a mosque and sits down without praying tahiyyat al-masjid?
A) The greeting is not nullified; he may pray later.
B) The greeting is invalid and cannot be made up.
C) He must perform a prostration of forgetfulness.
D) He should leave and re-enter to pray.

Key Takeaway
Tahiyyat al-masjid is a sunnah that can be fulfilled by any prayer upon entry, but it must be postponed if entry occurs during a disliked time.

Disliked Talk and Sleep in the Mosque
Ruling on talk and sleep in the mosque

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on worldly talk and sleeping in the mosque, and identify the condition that makes talk disliked.

Core Idea
Worldly talk is disliked only when one sits specifically for it; sleep is disliked because the mosque is for prayer.

This lesson delves into the Hanafi classification of two common activities in the mosque: conversation and sleep. The text from Al-Bahr al-Ra’iq clarifies that these are not absolutely prohibited but are disliked under specific conditions, guiding the believer to preserve the mosque's sanctity as a place of worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts under discussion
The passage addresses two separate actions: talking (kalam al-nas) and sleeping in the mosque. First, note that it refers to permissible worldly talk, not religious speech.
2
Determine the condition for dislike in talking
The ruling on talk is qualified: it is disliked only if one sits in the mosque specifically for the purpose of engaging in worldly conversation. If a person sits for worship and then talks incidentally, it is not disliked.
This distinction is from al-Zahiriyyah and Fath al-Qadir.
3
Establish the hukm for talking
The hukm is makruh (disliked). The text says it 'eats up good deeds,' indicating it reduces reward, but it is not haram.
The dislike is makruh tanzih (slightly disliked) in the Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the ruling on sleeping
For sleeping, the scholars differed. The preferred view (as per al-Tajnīs) is that it is disliked because the mosque is built exclusively for prayer, not for rest.
The disagreement among mashayikh is noted, but the relied-upon fatwa is dislike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصرح في الظهيرية بكراهة الحديث أي كلام الناس في المسجد لكن قيده بأن يجلس لأجله. وفي فتح القدير : الكلام المباح فيه مكروه يأكل الحسنات، وينبغي تقييده بما في الظهيرية. أما إن جلس للعبادة ثم بعدها تكلم فلا وأما النوم في المسجد فاختلف المشايخ فيه. وفي التجنيس : الأشبه بما تقدم من المسائل أنه يكره لأنه ما أعد لذلك وإنما بني لإقامة الصلاة، وأما الجلوس في المسجد للمصيبة فمكروه لأنه لم يبن له وعن الفقيه أبي الليث أنه لا بأس به لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حين بلغه قتل جعفر وزيد بن حارثة جلس في المسجد والناس يأتونه ويعزونه، والمفتي به أنه قوله : (قيده بأن يجلس لأجله قال في النهر والإطلاق أوجه قوله : (وصحح في مصلي العيد فوقه والبول والتخلي لا فوق بيت فيه مسجد ولا نقشه بالجص وماء الذهب. لا يلازم غريمه في المسجد لأن المسجد بني لذكر الله تعالى. ويجوز الجلوس في المسجد لغير الصلاة، ولا بأس به للقضاء كالتدريس والفتوى ا هـ . وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى بقية أحكام المسجد في الوقف والكراهية والجنايات ومسألة الذهاب إلى الأقدم أو إلى مسجد حيه أو إلى كان إمامه أصلح مذكورة في الخلاصة وغيرها بتفاريعها . من قوله : (لا فوق بيت فيه (مسجد) أي لا يكره ما ذكر في بيت فيه أو فوقه في ذلك البيت مسجد وهو مكان في البيت أعد للصلاة فإنه لم يأخذ حكم المسجد وإن كان يستحب للإنسان، رجلاً كان أو امرأة، أن يتخذ في داره مكاناً خالياً لصلاته وبه أمر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أصحابه.
And it is stated explicitly in al-Zahiriyyah that talking (i.e., people's speech) in the mosque is disliked, but he restricted it to sitting for its sake. And in Fath al-Qadir: permissible speech in it is disliked, it eats up good deeds, and it should be restricted to what is in al-Zahiriyyah. As for if he sits for worship and then talks after it, then no (dislike). As for sleeping in the mosque, the scholars (mashayikh) differed concerning it. In al-Tajnīs: the more appropriate given the preceding issues is that it is disliked because it was not prepared for that; rather it was built for establishing prayer. And as for sitting in the mosque for a calamity, it is disliked because it was not built for that, and from al-Faqih Abu al-Layth that there is no harm in it because the Prophet (peace be upon him) when news of the killing of Ja'far and Zayd ibn Haritha reached him, he sat in the mosque and people came to him and offered condolences, and the fatwa is according to his statement: (he restricted it to sitting for its sake). He said in al-Nahr: and the absolute permission is a valid viewpoint. His statement: (and it is corrected in the musalla of the 'Eid). Above it, and urination and relieving oneself are not (disliked) above a house in which there is a mosque, nor its decoration with plaster and liquid gold. He should not pursue his debtor in the mosque because the mosque was built for the remembrance of Allah Almighty. And it is permissible to sit in the mosque for other than prayer, and there is no harm in it for judging, like teaching and giving fatwa. And there will come, Allah willing, the remaining rulings of the mosque in the sections on waqf, disliked matters, and crimes, and the issue of going to the older (mosque) or to the mosque of his neighborhood or to (a mosque) whose imam is more righteous is mentioned in al-Khulasah and others with its branches. From his statement: ('not above a house in which there is a mosque') i.e., what is mentioned is not disliked in a house in which or above which in that house there is a mosque; it is a place in the house prepared for prayer, for it does not take the ruling of the mosque, although it is recommended for a person, male or female, to take a secluded place in his house for his prayer, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) commanded his companions to do so.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings regarding disliked acts in the mosque. First, worldly talk (kalam al-nas) is disliked if one sits specifically for that purpose; sitting for worship then talking is not disliked. Sleeping in the mosque is also disliked because it is not built for that purpose. Regarding sitting for mourning (for a calamity): it is disliked, but Abu al-Layth allowed it citing the Prophet's example. The relied-upon view restricts it to sitting for the sake of mourning, not for prolonged display of grief. The text then lists other disliked matters: pursuing a debtor in the mosque (because the mosque is for dhikr), and then explains that a prayer area in a house (even if dedicated) does not have the full ruling of a mosque—disliked acts like urination are not disliked there. However, it is recommended to have a designated prayer spot at home.

Key Terms
الكلام المباح (al-kalam al-mubah): Permissible worldly talk, which is still disliked if one sits specifically for it in the mosque.
النوم في المسجد (al-nawm fi al-masjid): Sleeping in the mosque; considered disliked by the relied-upon view because the mosque is built for prayer.

Key Takeaway
Worldly talk in the mosque is disliked only when one sits specifically for that purpose; sleeping is disliked because the mosque is built for prayer.

Disliked Mourning, Debtor Pursuit, and Private Prayer Room
Rulings on mourning, debtors, and private prayer room

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sitting for mourning in the mosque, the prohibition of pursuing debtors, and the status of a private prayer room.

Core Idea
Sitting for mourning is disliked unless brief; pursuing debtors is prohibited; a private prayer room does not take full mosque rulings.

This lesson continues with additional mosque etiquette: receiving condolences in the mosque, the prohibition of harassing debtors inside, and an important distinction between a full mosque and a dedicated prayer space at home. These rulings demonstrate how the sanctity of the mosque is preserved while making allowances for necessity.

Guided Path
1
Examine the ruling on sitting for a calamity
The text first states that sitting in the mosque to mourn a loss (al-julus li’l-musibah) is disliked because the mosque was not built for that. It then presents an opposing view from al-Faqih Abu al-Layth who permits it based on the Prophet's example. The relied-upon view is that it is disliked but with a qualification: it is only disliked if one sits specifically for that purpose.
The fatwa is according to the restricted view.
2
Understand the prohibition of pursuing debtors
The passage states: 'He should not pursue his debtor in the mosque because the mosque was built for the remembrance of Allah.' This implies a prohibition (haram or makruh tahrimi).
3
Analyze the ruling on a private prayer space (bayt fihi masjid)
A room designated for prayer in a house does not take the full ruling of a mosque. Therefore, acts like urination or decoration with plaster and gold are not disliked in such a space. The text clarifies that it is recommended to have a dedicated prayer spot at home.
4
Synthesize the overall theme
All these rulings stem from the principle that a mosque is built for worship and dhikr; any activity that distracts from that purpose is either disliked or prohibited. However, necessary activities like teaching, judging, or brief condolences are permitted.

The relied-upon view restricts it to sitting for the sake of mourning, not for prolonged display of grief. The text then lists other disliked matters: pursuing a debtor in the mosque (because the mosque is for dhikr), and then explains that a prayer area in a house (even if dedicated) does not have the full ruling of a mosque—disliked acts like urination are not disliked there. However, it is recommended to have a designated prayer spot at home.

Key Terms
الجلوس للمصيبة (al-julus li'l-musibah): Sitting in the mosque to receive condolences or mourn a loss, which is disliked unless brief and out of necessity.
ملازمة الغريم (mulazamat al-gharim): Following or harassing a debtor in the mosque to demand payment; prohibited because the mosque is for dhikr.
بيت فيه مسجد (bayt fihi masjid): A room in a house designated as a prayer space (musalla); it does not have the full sanctity of a mosque.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is permissible if done politely.
B) It is disliked but not sinful.
C) It is prohibited because the mosque is for dhikr.
D) It is recommended to remind the debtor of Allah.

Key Takeaway
Sitting for mourning is disliked unless brief; pursuing debtors is prohibited; a private prayer room does not have full mosque sanctity.

Legal Status of the Eid and Funeral Prayer Ground
Refining the ruling on the legal status of the Eid musalla

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the legal status of the Eid musalla and explain in which respect it is treated as a mosque.

Core Idea
The Eid musalla is a mosque only for joining prayer; for all other rules it is not a mosque.

We often treat Eid prayer grounds like mosques, but Hanafi jurists discuss whether they carry all mosque rulings. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq refines the position: the musalla is considered a mosque only for the validity of following the imam (iqtida') when rows are disconnected. For acts like impurity or entering in a state of major impurity, it is not treated as a mosque, out of leniency for people.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two initial views
First, note the view in al-Muhit that the funeral musalla has no mosque rulings at all. Similarly for the Eid musalla, with one exception.
The exception is for iqtida' (following the imam).
2
Understand the chosen fatwa in al-Nihayah
In al-Nihayah and other works, the preferred view is that a musalla built for Eid/funeral prayer is a mosque only for the purpose of joining prayer (iqtida'), even if rows are disconnected. This is out of leniency.
3
Analyze the author's critical refinement
The text then challenges the apparent view from al-Nihayah that acts like intercourse, urination, and defecation are permissible in the musalla. The author argues that since the place was built for prayer, these should not be allowed even if it is not a full mosque.
The author's critique shows the need to balance leniency with respect for the prayer space.
4
Summarize the practical implications
The main practical benefit: a junub (person in major impurity) or menstruating woman may enter the musalla, whereas they cannot enter a proper mosque. Also, following the imam is valid even if rows are separate.
This leniency facilitates attendance at large gatherings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في مصلى الجنازة والعيد فصحح في المحيط في مصلى الجنائز أنه ليس له حكم المسجد أصلاً، وصحح في مصلى العيد كذلك إلا في حق جواز الاقتداء وإن لم تتصل الصفوف. وفي النهاية وغيرها والمختار للفتوى في المسجد الذي اتخذ لصلاة الجنازة والعيد أنه مسجد في حق جواز الاقتداء وإن انفصل الصفوف رفقاً بالناس وفيما عدا ذلك ليس له حكم المسجد ا هـ وظاهر ما في النهاية أنه يجوز الوطء والبول والتخلي في مصلي الجنائز والعيد، ولا يخفى ما فيه فإن الباني لم يعده لذلك فينبغي أن لا ولا يخفي ما فيه فإن الباني لم لذلك فينبغي أن لا تجوز هذه الثلاثة وإن حكمنا بكونه غير مسجد، وإنما تظهر فائدته في بقية الأحكام التي ذكرناها ومن حل دخوله للجنب والحائض.
And they differed concerning the musalla (prayer ground) for the funeral and 'Eid. It is corrected in al-Muhit regarding the musalla for funerals that it does not have the ruling of a mosque at all. And it is corrected in the musalla for 'Eid similarly, except regarding the validity of following (the imam) even if the rows are not connected. And in al-Nihayah and others, the chosen for fatwa regarding the mosque that is taken for funeral and 'Eid prayer is that it is a mosque concerning the validity of following even if the rows are separate, out of leniency for the people, and in other than that it does not have the ruling of a mosque. And the apparent (view) in al-Nihayah is that intercourse, urine, and relieving oneself are permissible in the musalla for funerals and 'Eid, and it is not hidden what is in it, for the builder did not prepare it for that, so it should not... and it is not hidden what is in it, for the builder did not [prepare] for that, so it should not be permitted to do these three acts even if we rule it to be other than a mosque. Rather, its benefit appears in the remaining rulings that we have mentioned, and among them is permission for a junub or menstruating woman to enter.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage addresses an important question: do the open prayer grounds used for funeral prayers (musalla al-jana'iz) and Eid prayers (musalla al-'eid) have the same rules as a mosque? There is difference of opinion. According to al-Muhit, the funeral musalla has no mosque rulings. For the Eid musalla, it is similar, except that following the imam (iqtida') is valid even if rows are disconnected. However, in al-Nihayah, the selected fatwa is that if the place is designated as a 'masjid' (e.g., built for Eid prayer), it is considered a mosque specifically for joining prayer (iqtida') but not for other prohibitions like prohibition of impurity. The apparent view in al-Nihayah is that one may have intercourse, urinate, or defecate in the Eid and funeral musalla, but the author criticizes this: since it is built for prayer, these acts should not be permitted. The practical benefit of the difference is seen in other rules: for example, a person in major impurity (junub) or a menstruating woman may enter the musalla, which they cannot enter in a proper mosque.

Key Terms
مصلى العيد (musalla al-'eid): An open-air prayer ground designated for the two Eid prayers, often outside the city.
جواز الاقتداء (jawaz al-iqtida'): The validity of following an imam in prayer, which in a proper mosque requires connected rows; in the Eid musalla, it is valid even if rows are disconnected.
الوطء والبول والتخلي (al-wat' wa al-bawl wa al-takhalli): Intercourse, urination, and defecation — acts that are prohibited in a mosque but may be permissible in a musalla.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen fatwa in al-Nihayah, for which purpose is the Eid musalla considered a mosque?
A) For all rulings, like a regular mosque.
B) Only for the validity of following the imam (iqtida').
C) Only for performing Salah.
D) It is never considered a mosque.

Key Takeaway
The Eid musalla is a mosque only for the purpose of iqtida' (following the imam); for all other rulings, it is not a mosque, allowing leniencies like entry of junub and disconnected rows.

Ruling on Decorating Mosques with Plaster and Gold
A ruling statement on the legal status of mosque decoration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on decorating mosques with plaster and gold and explain why it is mubah (permissible but not recommended) rather than mustahabb or makruh.

Core Idea
Decorating a mosque is legally neutral — it brings no reward, and charity is better.

You may have seen beautiful mosques with intricate plaster and gold decorations and wondered about their religious merit. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position: such decoration is legally permissible but not meritorious. The key is understanding the phrase 'la ba's bihi' and why spending on the poor is preferred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the three scholarly positions
The act is decorating mosque walls with plaster (jiss) and gold leaf (ma' al-dhahab). Three views exist: 'no harm' (la ba's), dislike (karahah), and recommendation (istihbab). Your task is to note each position's basis.
The hadith about decoration being a sign of the Hour is cited by those who dislike it.
2
Analyze the linguistic clue 'la ba's'
The phrase 'la ba's' in al-Jami' al-Saghir indicates permissibility but not reward. According to al-Sarakhsi, it means the doer merely breaks even—no sin, no reward. Understand that in Hanafi usul, 'la ba's' often signals mubah, not mustahabb.
The term 'ba's' implies severity, so negating it removes blame.
3
Distinguish mubah from mustahabb in practice
The text explicitly says 'it is not recommended (la yustahabb)' and 'spending on the poor is more beloved (ahabbu)'. The superlative 'ahabbu' does not mean the decoration is recommended; rather, charity is simply a better use of funds. This is a critical refinement: the act is neutral, not virtuous.
Taj al-Shari'ah considered it recommended, but the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is mubah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا نقشه بالجص وماء الذهب) أي ولا يكره نقش المسجد وهو المذكور في الجامع الصغير بلفظ لا بأس به وقيل يكره للحديث إن من اشراط الساعة تزيين المساجد» وقيل مستحب من عمارته وقد مدح الله فاعلها بقوله إنما يعمر مساجد الله ﴾ [التوبة : لأنه كذلك يخالفه ما قاله تاج الشريعة والأصح أنه أي مصلي العيد يأخذ حكمها أي المساجد لأنه أعد لإقامة الصلاة فيه بالجماعة لأعظم الجموع على وجه الإعلان إلا أنه أبيح إدخال الدواب فيها ضرورة الخشية على ضياعها، وقد يجوز إدخال الدواب في بقعة المساجد لمكان العذر والضرورة اهـ. فقد اختلف التصحيح في مصلي العيد واتفق في مصلي الجنازة. كذا في الشرنبلالية قوله : (في حق بقية الأحكام التي ذكرناها أي كجواز الوضوء والمضمضة فيه ومسح الرجل من الطين بحشيشه والبصاق ونحو ذلك مما مر قوله : (وهو المذكور (الخ) قال في النهاية قال شمس الأئمة السرخسي رحمه الله تعالى : في قوله لا بأس إشارة إلى أنه لا يؤجر بذلك فيكفيه إن ينجو رأساً برأس اهـ . لأن في لا بأس دليلاً على أن المستحب غيره وإنما كان كذلك لأن البأس الشدة .اهـ. قلت : وفيه نفي لقول من جعله قربة لما فيه من تعظيم المسجد وإجلال الدين وبه صرح الزيلعي ثم قال : وعندنا لا بأس به ولا يستحب وصرفه إلى المساكين أحب .اهـ. وأفعل التفضيل ليس على بابه لأنه نفي استحباب صرفه لفظة.
His statement: (nor its decoration with plaster and liquid gold) i.e., the decoration of the mosque is not disliked. It is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir with the wording 'there is no harm in it.' And it is said that it is disliked because of the hadith 'Indeed from the signs of the Hour is the embellishment of the mosques.' And it is said that it is recommended as part of their construction, and Allah has praised those who do so by His saying: 'Only those who maintain the mosques of Allah' [al-Tawbah 9:18]. Because likewise, what Taj al-Shari'ah said contradicts it: the most correct is that the 'Eid musalla takes its (the mosques') ruling because it is prepared for establishing prayer in congregation for the greatest crowds in a public manner, except that it is permitted to bring riding beasts into them due to fear of losing them, and bringing beasts into the precincts of mosques may be permitted for a reason and necessity. Thus the correction differs on the 'Eid musalla, and agrees on the funeral musalla. Similarly in al-Shurnubalaliyyah. His statement: (regarding the rest of the rulings that we have mentioned) i.e., like the permissibility of performing ablution and rinsing the mouth in it, and wiping the foot from mud with its grass, and spitting, and similar things that have passed. His statement: (and it is the mentioned etc.) He said in al-Nihayah: Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: The phrase 'no harm' indicates that he is not rewarded for it; it suffices for him to break even, because in 'no harm' there is evidence that the recommended is other than it; and that is because 'bass' means severity. I say: And in it is a denial of the statement of those who made it a means of drawing near (to Allah) because of what it contains of magnifying the mosque and glorifying the religion. This is explicit from al-Zayla'i. Then he said: According to us, there is no harm in it and it is not recommended; and spending it on the poor is more beloved. And the superlative is not used in its real sense because it denies the recommendation of spending it on the word.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on decorating mosque walls with plaster (jiss) and gold. There are three views: (1) 'No harm' (la ba's) from al-Jami' al-Saghir, meaning it is permitted but not rewarded; (2) disliked, based on a hadith that mosque decoration is a sign of the Hour; (3) recommended, as it maintains the mosque's dignity. The author, citing al-Nihayah via al-Sarakhsi, explains that the phrase 'la ba's' implies that the doer is not rewarded—it is a neutral act. Al-Zayla'i explicitly denies that it is a form of worship (qurbah). The preferred Hanafi view is: it is not disliked (la ba's bihi) but also not recommended (la yustahabb); spending the money on the poor is better. The superlative 'ahabbu' (more beloved) does not mean the act is recommended, but simply that charity is preferable.

Key Terms
نقش المسجد (naqsh al-masjid): Decorating or embellishing the mosque, especially with plaster and gold leaf.
لا بأس به (la ba's bihi): An expression meaning 'there is no harm in it,' indicating permissibility without reward.
أحب (ahabbu): Comparative 'more beloved' used here not to indicate recommendation but to show preference for charity over decoration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view, what is the ruling on decorating mosques with plaster and gold?
A) It is recommended (mustahabb).
B) It is disliked (makruh).
C) It is prohibited (haram).
D) It is permissible but not recommended (mubah).

Key Takeaway
Mosque decoration is mubah – permissible but neither rewarded nor recommended; charity is the superior choice.

Witr: Wajib or Sunnah? The Internal Hanafi Dispute
Explains the ruling of Witr prayer and the internal Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on Witr (wajib vs sunnah mu'akkadah), identify the key proofs, and explain the practical consequences of the ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
Witr is wajib according to Abu Hanifa, but sunnah mu'akkadah according to the Sahibayn — a difference rooted in proof strength.

This lesson examines a significant intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: the legal status of Witr prayer. While Abu Hanifa considered it wajib (a binding obligation), his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarded it as an emphasized sunnah. The dispute is not merely theoretical — it leads to different rulings on qada' and the validity of the Fajr prayer if a missed Witr is remembered. Understanding this debate sharpens one's grasp of how Hanafi jurists weigh evidence (dalil) and how the category of 'wajib' functions between fard and sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and the ikhtilaf
Begin by noting the two positions: Abu Hanifa says Witr is wajib; Sahibayn say it is sunnah mu'akkadah. The text also records a reconciliation: 'fard in practice, wajib in creed, sunnah in establishment.' Understand that this is a dispute within the school, not between madhhabs.
The reconciliation attempts to acknowledge both views.
2
Analyze the proofs for each position
Abu Hanifa's proof: Hadith 'Witr is a right; whoever does not pray it is not of me' (Abu Dawud) and the command 'Pray witr and approach the morning' (Muslim). The imperative indicates wujub in Hanafi usul. Sahibayn's proof: Hadith of the Bedouin and the Prophet praying witr on his camel, suggesting it is not a fard/wajib.
The camel incident is a situational occurrence, not a general ruling.
3
Examine the Hanafi response to the opposing proofs
The author refutes the Sahibayn's proofs: the Bedouin hadith only refers to the five daily prayers; the camel incident could have been due to an excuse or before witr became wajib. The authoritative commands outweigh these.
The response uses usul principles like 'al-amr yufidu al-wujub'.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: If one remembers a missed Witr while praying Fajr, according to Abu Hanifa the Fajr is invalid (because a wajib is being remembered); according to Sahibayn, it is not invalidated. Also, qada' of missed Witr is required by Abu Hanifa but not by Sahibayn.
These consequences affect daily prayer practice.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and its authority
The text states that the last and correct position of Abu Hanifa is that Witr is wajib, supported by al-Muhit, al-Khaniyya, and al-Mabsut. This is the relied-upon view for fatwa.
Later Hanafi fatwa works follow Abu Hanifa's wajib position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوتر واجب وهو ثلاث ركعات بتسليمة وقنت في ثالثته قبل الركوع أبداً وقرأ الوتر . كذا في المغرب. وهو في الشرع صلاة مخصوصة وهي ثلاث ركعات بعد العشاء، والنفل في اللغة الزيادة وفي الشريعة زيادة عبادة شرعت لنا لا علينا، ووجوه اشتقاقه يدل على الزيادة ولهذا يسمى ولد الولد نافلة لأنه زيادة على الولد الصلبي وتسمى الغنيمة نفلاً لأنها زيادة على أصل المال قوله : الوتر واجب وهذا آخر أقوال أبي حنيفة وهو الصحيح. في المحيط. والأصح كما في الخانية وهو الظاهر من مذهبه كذا في المبسوط وروي عنه أنه سنة. ووفق المشايخ بينهما بأنه فرض عملاً واجب اعتقاداً، سنة ثبوتاً فرض وعنه ودليلاً. وأما عندهما فسنة عملاً واعتقاداً ودليلاً لكن سنة مؤكدة أكد من سائر السنن المؤقتة كما في البدائع لظهور أثر السنن فيه حيث لا يؤذن له ولم يثبت عندهما دليل الوجوب فنفياه . وأما استدلاله في الهداية لهما بأنه لا يكفر جاحده لا يفيد إذ إثبات منه ما رواه أبو داود مرفوعاً الوتر حق فمن لم يوتر فليس مني، الوتر حق فمن لم يوتر فليس مني، الوتر حق فمن لم يوتر فليس مني " رواه الحاكم وصححه . رواه مسلم مرفوعاً أو تر وأقبل أن تصبحوا (۲) والأمر للوجوب . وأما ما في الصحيحين أنه عليه السلام أو تر على بعيره فواقعة حال لا عموم لها فيجوز كونه كان للعذر من والاتفاق على أن الفرض يصلي على الدابة لعذر الطين والمرض ونحوه أو أنه كان قبل وجوبه وجوبه لم يقارن وجوب الخمس بل متأخر، وقد روي أنه عليه السلام كان ينزل للوتر . وأما حديث الأعرابي حين قال له هل علي غيرها أي الصلوت الخمس فقال له النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : لا إلا أن تطوع. فلا يدل على عدم وجوب الوتر كما زعمه النووي في شرح مسلم لأنه كان في لأن (1) وما صرحوا في المتون بالفرق وفرعوا على كل قول أحكاماً للآخر كفساد الفجر بتذكره وفساده بتذكر فرض قبله؟ اهـ . قلت : وهو عجيب ونقل العلامة الرملي له أعجب وكأن منشأه الغفلة عن قول المؤلف إلا في فساد الصبح «الخ) قوله : (إلا) في فساد الصبح وتذكره (الخ أي وإلا في عدم إعادته لو ظهر فساد العشاء دونه عنده لا عندهما قال في المنظومة : والوتر فرض ویری بذكره ولا يعاد الوتر إذ يعاد في فجره فساد فرض فجره عشاؤه إن ظهر الفساد اهـ
Witr is obligatory, and it is three rak'ahs with one salam, and the qunut is recited in the third rak'ah before bowing always, and he recited the witr. Similarly in al-Mughrib. And in sacred law it is a specific prayer: three rak'ahs after Isha. And nafal in language is increase, and in sacred law it is an increase of worship legislated for us but not upon us. The aspects of its derivation indicate increase, hence the child of a child is called nafilah because it is an increase over the direct child, and spoils of war are called nafal because they are an increase over original wealth. His statement: 'Witr is obligatory' and this is the last of Abu Hanifa's opinions, and it is the correct one according to al-Muhit. And the most correct as in al-Khaniyya, and it is the apparent from his school, like in al-Mabsut, and it is related from him that it is sunnah. And the scholars reconciled between them by saying: it is obligatory (fard) in practice, obligatory (wajib) in creed, sunnah in establishment, fard from him, and it is a proof. As for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is sunnah in practice, creed, and proof, but an emphasized sunnah, more emphasized than other time-bound sunnahs as in al-Bada'i' because the effect of sunnah appears in it since no adhan is given for it, and no proof of obligation was established for them so they negated it. And as for his argument in al-Hidayah for them that its denier is not deemed an unbeliever, it is not useful because proving the necessary does not entail proving the specific necessary unless it equals it, and here it is more general; and indeed non-deeming of unbelief by denial is a necessary of obligation as it is a necessary of sunnah, and the claim is obligation not fard. And as for the Imam, the proof of obligation was established for him, and that is the hadith, the best of which is what Abu Dawud narrated marfu': 'Witr is a right; whoever does not pray witr is not of me...' narrated by al-Hakim and authenticated. Muslim narrated marfu': 'Pray witr and approach the morning' and the command indicates obligation. And as for what is in the two Sahihs that he (peace be upon him) prayed witr on his camel, it is a situational occurrence with no general applicability; it could have been due to an excuse, and there is consensus that the obligatory prayer is performed on an animal due to an excuse of mud, illness, or similar, or that it was before its obligation; its obligation did not coincide with the obligation of the five prayers but was later. It has been related that he (peace be upon him) used to dismount for witr. And as for the hadith of the Bedouin when he said to him: 'Is there anything else upon me besides the five prayers?' and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'No, unless you volunteer.' This does not indicate the non-obligation of witr as al-Nawawi claimed in his commentary on Muslim, because it was in... because... (1) And what they stated in the texts about the difference and they derived rulings for each saying for the other, like the corruption of Fajr due to remembering it and its corruption due to remembering a previous fard? etc. He said: It is strange, and the transmission of al-Ramli is stranger, and it seems the source is neglect of the author's statement except in corruption of Subh... His statement: (except) in corruption of Subh and remembering it... meaning except also in not repeating it if the corruption of Isha appears without it according to him not according to them. He said in the poem: 'And witr is fard, and it is seen by its remembrance, and witr is not repeated when it is repeated in its Fajr, corruption of its Fajr fard, its Isha if corruption appears.' end.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq is an expansion on the ruling of Witr prayer. It opens with a definition: Witr is a specific prayer of three rak'ahs after Isha with one salam and qunut before ruku'. It then defines nafilah as an increase in worship that is recommended but not obligatory. The core discussion is the legal status of Witr. According to Abu Hanifa, Witr is wajib (obligatory). This is his final position and the correct one in the school. However, a narration from him says it is sunnah. Scholars reconcile this by saying it is 'fard in practice, wajib in creed, sunnah in establishment'—meaning its obligation is less than that of the five daily prayers. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it is sunnah, though an emphasized sunnah, because no proof of obligation (dalil al-wujub) was established for them. The passage then presents the proofs: Abu Hanifa relies on hadith such as 'Witr is a right; whoever does not pray it is not of me' (Abu Dawud) and the command 'Pray witr and approach the morning' (Muslim), which indicates obligation. The Sahibayn's view is supported by the fact that the Prophet prayed witr on his camel (a situational case) and the hadith of the Bedouin where the Prophet said no further obligations beyond the five prayers. The passage refutes the counterarguments, showing that the evidence for obligation is stronger. Finally, it notes practical differences: for example, if one remembers a missed Witr while praying Fajr, the Fajr prayer becomes invalid according to Abu Hanifa but not according to the Sahibayn, as stated in the poem. This intra-Hanafi dispute has real consequences in fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
الوتر (al-witr): A specific prayer of three rak'ahs after Isha, with one salam and qunut before ruku' in the third rak'ah.
النفل (al-nafl): Supererogatory worship; increase over obligatory acts.
الوجوب (al-wujub): Obligation, a legal category between fard and sunnah for Hanafis, based on zanni evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding Witr?
A) According to Abu Hanifa, a missed Witr requires qada'; according to Sahibayn, it does not.
B) According to Abu Hanifa, the Witr prayer is invalid if prayed in congregation; according to Sahibayn, it is valid.
C) According to Abu Hanifa, Witr is prayed after the night prayer; according to Sahibayn, it can be prayed anytime.
D) There is no practical difference; both agree on the same rulings.

Key Takeaway
Witr is wajib according to Abu Hanifa's final position, which is the mufta bihi, and the practical difference from the Sahibayn's view lies in qada' and the validity of Fajr when a missed Witr is remembered.

The Legal Status of Witr Prayer: Wajib or Sunnah?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the legal status of witr prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa considers witr wajib while the Sahibayn consider it sunnah muakkadah, and identify the practical difference in rulings (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Witr is wajib according to Abu Hanifa but sunnah muakkadah according to the Sahibayn — the debate hinges on the interpretative weight of hadith commands.

The witr prayer is a nightly prayer that holds a unique status in the Hanafi school. While Abu Hanifa considers it a binding obligation (wajib), his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, view it as a highly emphasized sunnah. This difference is not merely theoretical — it affects practical rulings such as the permissibility of making up witr at certain times. In this lesson, we will explore the evidence, reconcile the views, and see how the school ultimately resolves the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the disputants
First, note that the passage clearly states Abu Hanifa's final position: witr is wajib. The two companions differ, holding it as sunnah muakkadah. Recognize that this is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's dalil
Examine the hadith cited: 'الوتر حق فمن لم يوتر فليس مني' and 'أوتروا وأقبل أن تصبحوا'. In Hanafi usul, the word 'حق' and the imperative 'أوتروا' indicate obligation (wujub). Consider that the evidence is zanni al-thubut (ahad hadith), so it does not reach the level of fard.
The dalil is ahad, hence the hukm is wajib, not fard.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's counter-arguments
The Sahibayn rely on the hadith of the Bedouin (no obligation beyond the five) and the fact that the Prophet prayed witr on his camel. They interpret the command as emphasis, not obligation. Understand their reasoning that the absence of takfir of the denier suggests it is not wajib.
The Sahibayn argue the command is for recommendation (nadb) not obligation.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The passage refutes the Bedouin hadith by noting it was before witr became obligatory. The camel hadith is a specific situation (excuse or before obligation). The lack of takfir does not prove non-obligation because even denial of a sunnah does not cause takfir, but denial of a wajib generally does not either; the point is that the denier of a wajib is not necessarily a disbeliever, so the absence of takfir is neutral.
The Hanafi response uses contextual and usul arguments to preserve the obligation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical differences: (1) Qada of witr after Fajr and Asr: Abu Hanifa permits it (like fard), Sahibayn do not (since it is sunnah). (2) Witr on a mount without excuse: not allowed by any, but for nafl it is allowed by Sahibayn. (3) Community abandoning witr: Abu Hanifa would discipline them, Sahibayn would not. These show the impact of the dispute.
The thamarah is essential for understanding how the ikhtilaf affects daily practice.

Book of Prayer, Chapter of Witr and Voluntary Prayers. Witr is obligatory and it is three rak'ahs with one taslim and qunut in the third before bowing always, and recitation in witr. Thus in al-Mughrib. It is in the Shari'ah a specific prayer, which is three rak'ahs after Isha. Nafilah in language means increase, and in Shari'ah it is an increase of worship prescribed for us not upon us, and its derivations indicate increase, hence the grandchild is called nafilah because it is an increase upon the direct child, and booty is called nafal because it is an increase upon the original wealth. His statement: 'Witr is obligatory' and this is the last statement of Abu Hanifah and it is the correct one. In al-Muhit. And the most correct as in al-Khaniyyah and it is the apparent from his madhhab, thus in al-Mabsut, and it is narrated from him that it is sunnah. The scholars reconciled between them that it is farz in practice, wajib in belief, sunnah in establishment, and farz in narration and proof. As for the two companions, it is sunnah in practice, belief, and proof, but a strongly emphasized sunnah, more emphasized than other fixed sunnahs as in al-Bada'i' due to the apparent effect of sunnah in it where no adhan is given for it and the proof of obligation is not established in their view so they negated it. As for his inference in al-Hidayah for them that its denier is not a disbeliever, it does not benefit because establishing the necessary does not entail establishing the specific necessary unless it is equivalent, and it is more general here. And the non-disbelief of its denier is a necessary of obligation just as it is a necessary of sunnah, and the claimant claims obligation not farz. As for the Imam, the proof of obligation is established with him, which is the hadith, and the best of what is specified from it is what Abu Dawud narrated as marfu': 'Witr is a right, so whoever does not pray witr is not from me' (repeated thrice), narrated by Al-Hakim and he authenticated it. Muslim narrated as marfu': 'Pray witr and hasten to dawn' and the command is for obligation. As for what is in the two Sahihs that he (peace be upon him) prayed witr on his camel, it is a situational occurrence without general application, so it is possible that it was due to an excuse, and the agreement that the farz is prayed on a mount due to the excuse of mud, illness, etc., or that it was before its obligation, and its obligation did not coincide with the obligation of the five but was later, and it is narrated that he (peace be upon him) used to dismount for witr. As for the hadith of the Bedouin when he said to him: 'Is there anything else upon me besides the five prayers?' and the Prophet said: 'No, unless you volunteer.' This does not indicate the non-obligation of witr as al-Nawawi claimed in his commentary on Muslim, because it was at the beginning of Islam, then witr became obligatory after that, evidenced by the fact that he asked about financial worship and he informed him about zakat, and he said: 'Is there anything else upon me?' and he said: 'No.' As mentioned in prayer, while sadaqat al-fitr is obligatory in their view with its proof, so whatever their answer is for it, it is our answer for it, and saying it is obligatory does not necessitate an increase over the five definite farz because it is not a definite farz. And in al-Bada'i' a story is mentioned: Yusuf ibn Khalid al-Sami, one of the prominent fuqaha of Basra, asked Abu Hanifah about it and he said: 'It is wajib.' He said: 'You have disbelieved, O Abu Hanifah,' thinking that he meant fard. So Abu Hanifah said: 'Does your declaring me a disbeliever frighten me while I know the difference between fard and wajib like the difference between heaven and earth?' Then he explained the difference to him, so he apologized and sat with him to learn. In al-Muhit: It is not permissible to pray witr sitting while able to stand, nor on one's mount without an excuse, because according to him witr is wajib and performing wajibat and fara'id on a mount without an excuse is not permissible, while according to them although it is sunnah, it is authentically reported that the Prophet used to pray nafl on his mount without an excuse at night, and when he reached witr he would dismount and pray witr on the ground. So it indicates that it is not permissible sitting or riding without an excuse by agreement of Abu Hanifah and his two companions. And it is stated in al-Hidayah that it must be made up if missed by consensus, and it is authenticated in al-Tajnis, and he gave the reason in al-Muhit: According to him because it is wajib, and according to them due to his statement: 'Whoever sleeps through witr or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers it.' And it is stated in al-Kafi that the obligation of its qada is the apparent narration from them, and it is narrated from them its non-obligation, and it will come that one does not pray behind a nafl by agreement. So it appears from this that there is no difference between his statement of its obligation and their statement of its sunnah in terms of rulings, for the strongly emphasized sunnah is in the position of wajib except in the corruption of Fajr due to remembering it and in its qada after dawn before sunrise. He said in al-Tajnis: According to Abu Hanifah, he makes it up after dawn before sunrise and after Asr prayer because it is wajib in his view, so its qada is permissible in these times like the qada of other fara'id, while according to them it is not because it is sunnah in their view. But the author of al-Hidayah objected in Fath al-Qadir that it is sunnah according to them, so the obligation of qada is a point of dispute, and its refutation has been known from what is in al-Muhit. In al-Dhahiriyyah, al-Walwaljiyyah, al-Tajnis, and others: If the people of a village agree on abandoning witr, the Imam disciplines them and imprisons them; if they do not desist, he fights them. Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion probe on the ruling of witr prayer. It opens by defining witr linguistically and legally, then states the core issue: the disagreement between Abu Hanifah and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The author first presents Abu Hanifah's final position that witr is wajib, and notes that this is the correct and apparent view in the madhhab, though a narration attributes sunnah to him. The scholars resolved this by saying it is farz in practice, wajib in belief, sunnah in establishment. For the two companions, witr is a strongly emphasized sunnah. The passage then discusses proofs: Abu Hanifah relies on hadith such as 'Witr is a right...' and 'Pray witr and hasten to dawn' which indicate obligation. Objections from hadith of the Bedouin and witr on camel are addressed. A story of Yusuf al-Sami illustrates the distinction between fard and wajib. The practical implications are then detailed: witr cannot be performed sitting or on mount without excuse by agreement; qada (making up) is obligatory by consensus according to al-Hidayah, though the two companions differ on its sunnah status. The exception to the equivalence of sunnah muakkadah and wajib is the corruption of Fajr and the timing of qada. The passage also mentions that a community abandoning witr may be disciplined. Overall, the passage establishes the Hanafi position firmly, while acknowledging the nuances within the school.

Key Terms
الوتر (al-witr): A specific prayer of three rak'ahs performed after Isha, with qunut in the third rak'ah.
الواجب (al-wajib): A binding obligation established by a zanni (probable) evidence, distinct from fard which is established by qat'i evidence.
السنة المؤكدة (al-sunnah al-mu'akkadah): A highly emphasized recommended action, nearly obligatory in practice but not in belief.
القضاء (al-qada'): Making up a missed obligatory or wajib prayer outside its prescribed time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on Abu Hanifa's view that witr is wajib, but the Sahibayn's view as sunnah muakkadah influences the permissibility of qada in certain times.

Salah — Prayer

The Obligation of Witr and the Fard-Wajib Distinction
Establishes witr as wajib and distinguishes it from fard using evidence and a story.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on witr prayer and explain why it is classified as wajib rather than fard, citing the evidence from the passage.

Core Idea
Witr is wajib—a binding obligation but not fard—because its evidence is zanni (presumptive), not qat'i (definitive).

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes the obligation of witr and then clarifies the crucial Hanafi distinction between fard and wajib. You will learn not only the legal ruling but also the epistemological basis that sets the Hanafi school apart. A famous story of Imam Abu Hanifa's encounter with a scholar drives the point home.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on witr
The passage opens by stating that witr became wajib after the initial obligations of Islam. Determine the explicit ruling: witr is wajib, not fard.
Notice that the passage uses 'وجب' (became obligatory) immediately.
2
Examine the primary evidence
The evidence cited is the hadith where the Prophet (s) was asked about financial worship and mentioned only zakat, answering 'No' when asked if there were other obligations. This implies no other financial worship is fard. Witr is analogized to this.
The analogy is that just as no other financial worship is fard, witr is not fard but wajib by other evidence.
3
Analyze the refutation of an objection
The text addresses a potential objection: if sadaqat al-fitr is considered fard by some (e.g., Shafi'is) with their evidence, then the same reasoning would apply to witr. The Hanafi response is that their answer about sadaqat al-fitr is the same as ours about witr: it is not fard.
This shows the consistency of the Hanafi principle.
4
Understand the fard-wajib distinction
The passage emphasizes that saying witr is obligatory does not add to the five definitive pillars because it is not a definitive obligation (qat'i). In Hanafi usul, fard is qat'i, wajib is zanni.
This is the core epistemological distinction.
5
Learn from the story of Yusuf ibn Khalid
The story illustrates the gravity of the distinction: Yusuf accused Abu Hanifa of disbelief thinking he considered witr a fard. Abu Hanifa responded that the difference is like heaven and earth, then explained it.
This anecdote reinforces the importance of precise legal terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أول الإسلام ثم وجب الوتر بعده بدليل أنه سأله عن العبادة المالية فأخبره بالزكاة فقال هل علي غيرها؟ فقال : لا . كما ذكر في الصلاة مع أن صدقة الفطر فرض عندهم بدليله فما هو جوابهم عنها فهو جوابنا عنه ولا يلزم من القول بوجوبه الزيادة على الفرائض الخمس القطعية لأنه ليس بفرض قطعي . ما وذكر في البدائع حكاية هي : أن يوسف بن خالد السمي كان من أعيان فقهاء البصرة فسأل أبا حنيفة عنه فقال : إنه واجب. فقال له كفرت يا أبا حنيفة ظناً منه أنه يقول إنه فريضة. فقال أبو حنيفة : أيهولني إكفارك إياي وأنا أعرف الفرق بين الفرض والواجب كفرق بین السماء والأرض، ثم بين له الفرق بينهما فاعتذر إليه وجلس عنده للتعلم اهـ.
At the beginning of Islam, then witr became obligatory after it, with the evidence that he (the Prophet) was asked about financial worship and he informed him of zakat, saying: 'Is there anything else upon me?' He said: 'No.' As mentioned in prayer, although sadaqat al-fitr is fard according to them with its evidence, so whatever their answer to it is, that is our answer about it. And the claim of its obligation does not necessitate an increase upon the five definitive obligations because it is not a definitive obligation. And it is mentioned in al-Bada'i' a story: that Yusuf ibn Khalid al-Samti was from the prominent jurists of Basra, so he asked Abu Hanifa about it, and he said: 'It is wajib.' So he said to him: 'You have disbelieved, O Abu Hanifa,' thinking that he said it is a fard. So Abu Hanifa said: 'Does your takfir of me terrify me while I know the difference between fard and wajib like the difference between heaven and earth?' Then he explained the difference between them, so he apologized to him and sat with him to learn. End.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and a biographical anecdote. It begins by stating that witr became obligatory after the initial obligations of Islam, using the famous hadith where the Prophet (s) informed the questioner that only zakat is obligatory as financial worship, and nothing else. This is analogous to the case of witr. The author then addresses an objection: if sadaqat al-fitr is considered fard by some (like the Shafi'is) with their evidence, then whatever their answer is regarding it, the Hanafis give the same answer for the obligation of witr—namely, that it is not fard but wajib. The key point is that saying witr is obligatory does not add to the five definitive obligations (the pillars) because it is not a definitive obligation (qat'i). Then the story of Yusuf ibn Khalid illustrates this distinction: he mistakenly thought Abu Hanifa considered witr a fard and accused him of disbelief. Abu Hanifa responded that the difference is as vast as between heaven and earth, and then taught him the correct distinction. In Hanafi fiqh, fard is established by qat'i evidence (Quran or mutawatir sunnah), while wajib is established by zanni evidence (e.g., ahad hadith). Witr belongs to the latter category.

Key Terms
الوتر (al-witr): The odd-numbered prayer performed after the night prayer, consisting of three rak'ahs according to the Hanafi school.
الفرض (al-fard): An obligation established by definitive evidence (qat'i), such as the five daily prayers, zakat, etc.
الواجب (al-wajib): An obligation established by presumptive evidence (zanni), such as witr and sadaqat al-fitr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is witr not considered a fard (definitive obligation)?
A) Because it is only recommended.
B) Because its evidence is not definitive (qat'i).
C) Because it was prescribed later in Islam.
D) Because the Prophet did not perform it regularly.

Key Takeaway
Witr is wajib in the Hanafi school, not fard, and the distinction between fard and wajib is based on the certainty of the evidence.
